201 of 2470 DOCUMENTS


State v. D. R.


Nos. A-12, A-13


Supreme Court of New Jersey


109 N.J. 348; 537 A.2d 667; 1988 N.J. LEXIS 3


September 29, 1987, Argued  
February 9, 1988, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] On certification to the Superior Court, Appellate Division, whose opinion is reported at 214 N.J. Super. 278 (1986).


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant sought review from the Superior Court, Appellate Division (New Jersey), which held that defendant's conviction for endangering the welfare of a child, N.J. Stat. Ann. § 2C:24-4(a), should have been merged with his convictions for aggravated sexual assault and sexual assault, N.J. Stat. Ann. § 2C:14-2(a) and (b). Defendant contended that the child's out-of-court account of the assault was hearsay and should have been inadmissible.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was convicted of aggravated sexual assault, N.J. Stat. Ann. § 2C:14-2(a), sexual assault, N.J. Stat. Ann. § 2C:14-2(b), and endangering the welfare of a child, N.J. Stat. Ann. § 2C:24-4(a), and sentenced to concurrent sentences. The appellate court affirmed the convictions but determined that the conviction for endangering the welfare should have been merged with the other convictions. The appellate court adopted an exception to the hearsay rule that authorized admissibility of a child's out-of-court statements concerning sexual abuse if made to a parent, confidant, physician, or other professional under circumstances that afforded sufficient indicia of reliability to justify admission. The court held that such a fundamental change in the hearsay rule solely by judicial decision was inconsistent with the procedure requiring collaboration among all three branches of government set forth in the Evidence Act, N.J. Stat. Ann. § 2A:84A-33 to -45. The court held that the adoption of such a rule was plain error because it was capable of producing an unjust result. The court reversed a judgment of the appellate court and remanded.


OUTCOME: The court reversed and remanded a judgment of the appellate court, which affirmed the trial court's convictions of aggravated sexual assault, sexual assault, and endangering the welfare of a child. The court held that the appellate court's adoption of an exception to the hearsay rule allowing admissibility of a child's out-of-court statements concerning sexual abuse was inconsistent with procedure set forth in the Evidence Act.

 

202 of 2470 DOCUMENTS


In re Baby M


No. A-39


SUPREME COURT OF NEW JERSEY


109 N.J. 396; 537 A.2d 1227; 1988 N.J. LEXIS 1; 77 A.L.R.4th 1


September 14, 1987, Argued  
February 3, 1988, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] On certification to the Superior Court, Chancery Division, Family Part, Bergen County, whose opinion is reported at 217 N.J. Super. 313 (1987).

Charles R. Gordon, M.D., Witness, Susan Feldman Gordon, Esq., Witness

William Stern, Natural Father

By: Notary Public PRIMARY RESEARCH ASSOCIATES OF UNITED STATES, INC. d/b/a INFERTILITY CENTER OF NEW YORK.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant surrogate mother challenged the order of the Superior Court (New Jersey) that enforced the terms of a surrogate parent contract on behalf of plaintiff father. Defendant claimed that the surrogacy contract was invalid; that the trial court improperly terminated her parental rights and awarded sole custody to plaintiff father, and improperly allowed the adoption of the child by plaintiff's wife, all pursuant to the terms of the contract.


OVERVIEW: On direct certification from the trial court, the court reversed the order that terminated defendant surrogate mother's parental rights, restored her as the mother of the child, and invalidated the surrogacy contract entered into between plaintiff father and defendant. The court ruled that the contract was invalid as a matter of law, because it violated statute and was against public policy. The court found that private placement adoption was disfavored, that the payment of money made the contract illegal and possibly criminal under N.J. Stat. Ann. § 9:3-54, and that it was vested with an element of coercion. The court ruled that the agreement was totally unenforceable. The court determined that the statute required a surrender of the child to a public agency and then a termination proceeding, which could only proceed after counseling. The court held that the termination of defendant's parental rights called for by the surrogacy contract and ordered by the trial court, failed to comply with statutory requirements. The court ruled that no one can contractually abandon one's parental rights. The court held that because the termination was invalid, the adoption was invalid.


OUTCOME: The court invalidated the surrogacy contract because it conflicted with the law and public policy of the state. The court granted custody to plaintiff father, voided both the termination of the surrogate mother's parental rights and the adoption of the child by plaintiff's wife, and restored defendant surrogate mother as the mother of the child. The court remanded the case on the issue of visitation only.

 

203 of 2470 DOCUMENTS


Kirk v. Newark


Nos. A-140, A-141


Supreme Court of New Jersey


109 N.J. 173; 536 A.2d 229; 1988 N.J. LEXIS 8


February 17, 1987, Argued  
January 25, 1988, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] On certification to the Superior Court, Appellate Division, whose opinion is reported at 212 N.J. Super. 201 (1986).


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant police officer challenged a judgment of the Superior Court, Appellate Division (New Jersey), reversing a judgment not withstanding the verdict in favor of respondent individual in an action claiming appellant violated respondent's civil rights brought under 42 U.S.C.S. § 1983.


OVERVIEW: Appellant police officer investigated a potential child abuse claim. An assistant county prosecutor advised appellant that probable cause existed to arrest respondent individual. Respondent filed a complaint under 42 U.S.C.S. § 1983, claiming the arrest was without probable cause and violated his constitutional rights. The trial court entered a judgment for appellant notwithstanding the verdict and the appellate court reversed. The court held that an officer was entitled to judgment as a matter of law when she could reasonably have believed in the existence of probable cause. Because appellant was confronted with a possible case of child abuse and acted on the advice of counsel, the court concluded as a matter of law that a reasonable police officer could have believed that she had probable cause to arrest. Because the law did not require an officer, who reasonably believed she had probable cause to exercise due diligence before an arrest, the court held that respondent failed to defeat appellant's assertion of qualified immunity. The court reversed the judgment of the appellate court and reinstated the judgment dismissing the complaint notwithstanding the verdict.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the judgment of the appellate court reversing a judgment notwithstanding the verdict and reinstated the judgment dismissing respondent's complaint because appellant police officer was afforded qualified immunity by the fact that she could reasonably have believed she had probable cause to arrest respondent and she was not required to exercise due diligence before the arrest.

 

204 of 2470 DOCUMENTS


Exxon Corp. v. Hunt


No. A-158


Supreme Court of New Jersey


109 N.J. 110; 534 A.2d 1; 1987 N.J. LEXIS 376; 18 ELR 20412; 26 ERC (BNA) 1953


February 17, 1987, Argued  
December 2, 1987, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] On remand from the Supreme Court of the United States, whose opinion is reported at 475 U.S.    , 106 S.Ct. 1103, 89 L.Ed.2d 364 (1986).


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff taxpayers sought refund of taxes paid under the New Jersey Spill Compensation and Control Act (Act), N.J. Stat. Ann. §§ 58:10-23.11 to 58:10-23.24, and challenged the findings and recommendations of the Tax Court (New Jersey). Plaintiff urged that the Act's provisions not pre-empted by the Comprehensive Environmental Response, Compensation, and Liability Act of 1980, 42 U.S.C.S. §§ 9601-9675, were not severable from the Act.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiff taxpayers claimed that non-offending portions of the New Jersey Spill Compensation and Control Act (Spill Act), N.J. Stat. Ann. §§ 58:10-23.11 to 58:10-23.24, that were not pre-empted by the Comprehensive Environmental Response, Compensation, and Liability Act of 1980 (CERCLA), 42 U.S.C.S. §§ 9601-9675, were not severable from the offending portions of the Spill Act, and that they were unenforceable. The court held that the purposes of the Spill Act held to be pre-empted could be severed from the remainder of the statute without impairing its validity. The court ruled that the Spill Act made sense with or without the pre-empted purposes. The court determined that the period of preemption ended on the effective date of the statute that repealed the preemption language of CERCLA. The court remanded the case for the lower court to determine what portion of the tax revenues were generated by severed portions of the Spill Act.


OUTCOME: The court remanded the case to the lower court for it to determine what portions of the tax revenues collected from plaintiff taxpayers were generated from the pre-empted portions of the act. The court ordered that the legislature was to reimburse the Spill Fund for lost monies, or to refund in full the taxes paid by plaintiff taxpayers to defendant, state environmental clean-up fund.

 

205 of 2470 DOCUMENTS


State v. Breakiron


No. A-105


Supreme Court of New Jersey


108 N.J. 591; 532 A.2d 199; 1987 N.J. LEXIS 371


January 20, 1987, Argued  
October 29, 1987, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] On appeal from and certification to Superior Court, Appellate Division, whose opinion is reported at 210 N.J. Super. 442 (1986).


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant challenged the order of the Superior Court (New Jersey), which affirmed his conviction for murder. Defendant claimed that the trial court erred when it refused to charge the jury that evidence of diminished capacity resulting from a mental disease or defect was relevant to the question of whether defendant's admitted killing of another was knowing or purposeful. Defendant challenged the constitutionality of N.J. Stat. Ann. § 2C:4-2.


OVERVIEW: At trial, defendant offered psychiatric testimony that he suffered from a disease or defect that rendered him unable to formulate the requisite mental state for murder. The court reversed the judgment and held that diminished capacity was evidence which denied the existence of facts which the state had to prove to establish the essential elements of first degree murder, that the allocation of responsibility between judge and jury required that competent reliable evidence be submitted to the jury and that a diminished capacity charge be given to the jury when competent reliable evidence had been offered. The court ruled that the defense of diminished capacity was not an affirmative defense that justified or excused conduct otherwise criminal, but rather allowed the introduction of evidence relevant to the question of whether the state had proven beyond a reasonable doubt the requisite criminal mental state. The court held that it was constitutionally permissible to require defendant to establish the presence of such mental disease or defect so long as the state retained the burden of proving the essential elements of the crime beyond a reasonable doubt.


OUTCOME: The court held that defendant was entitled to have the jury charged that relevant evidence of mental disease or defect could be considered either with respect to an insanity defense or as negating the state of mind required for murder. The judgment of the appellate court was reversed as to the murder conviction, affirmed as to companion convictions, and the matter remanded for further proceedings.

 

206 of 2470 DOCUMENTS


P.T. & L. Constr. Co. v. State, Dep't of Transp.


No. A-99/100


Supreme Court of New Jersey


108 N.J. 539; 531 A.2d 1330; 1987 N.J. LEXIS 368


January 5, 1987, Argued  
October 19, 1987, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] As Amended December 1, 1987.


PRIOR HISTORY: On certification to the Appellate Division, Superior Court.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant Department of Transportation sought review from the Appellate Division, Superior Court (New Jersey), which affirmed the judgment of the trial court awarding plaintiff construction corporation damages for delays caused by defendant in plaintiff's breach of contract claim against defendant. The appellate court upheld the award in all respects except as to the exculpatory clause in the contract.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiff construction corporation and defendant Department of Transportation executed public contract for highway work. Poor working conditions caused numerous delays, and plaintiff initiated action to recover damages. The trial court held that defendant was liable for material misrepresentations of the working conditions which caused the delays and that such misrepresentations were not covered by the exculpatory or no-damage-for-delay clauses. The appellate court affirmed in all respects except that it upheld the exculpatory clause as efficacious in the circumstances. The court held that facts were sufficient to support the finding that defendant's nondisclosure of material facts constituted a misrepresentation of conditions for which recovery should have been allowed. Pursuant to the New Jersey Contractual Liability Act, N.J. Stat. Ann. § 59:13-1 to -10, when the state made false representations it was liable for damages resulting despite general disclaimer of liability; however, if such disclaimer was sufficiently specific or if it only purported to be test results, a contractor could not recover. The court affirmed a judgment of the appellate court.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed a judgment of the appellate court, which affirmed a judgment of the trial court that defendant Department of Transportation was liable to plaintiff construction corporation for material misrepresentations of working conditions in public contract except for such damages that were covered under the exculpatory clause. The court held that the general disclaimer of liability did not cover defendant's false representations.

 

207 of 2470 DOCUMENTS


First Family Mortg. Corp. v. Durham


No. A-39


Supreme Court of New Jersey


108 N.J. 277; 528 A.2d 1288; 1987 N.J. LEXIS 357


October 7, 1986, Argued  
August 4, 1987, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] On Appeal from the Superior Court, Appellate Division, whose opinion is reported at 205 N.J. Super. 251 (1985).


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant foreign mortgage corporation sought review from the Superior Court, Appellate Division (New Jersey), which affirmed the trial court's dismissal of appellant's mortgage foreclosure suit because appellant failed to file a Notice of Business Activities Report as required by N.J. Stat. Ann. § 14A:13-15.


OVERVIEW: Appellant foreign mortgage corporation challenged the appellate court's judgment affirming the trial court's dismissal of appellant's mortgage foreclosure suit because appellant failed to file a Notice of Business Activities Report as required by N.J. Stat. Ann. § 14A:13-15. It was undisputed that appellant satisfied the conditions that would bar it from maintaining the action under the Corporation Business Activities Reporting Act, N.J. Stat. Ann. § 14A:13-14 to -23 (act), and that none of the exceptions applied. The court held that N.J. Stat. Ann. § 14A:13-20b, the enforcement provision, was unconstitutional. However, the court held that the unconstitutionality of the provision as written did not require invalidation of the entire act. The court concluded that to preserve the constitutionality of § 14A:13-20b, the statute was to be interpreted to mean that a foreign corporation could pursue a cause of action in the state after filing a report and meeting the conditions of N.J. Stat. Ann. § 14A:13-20c(2), regardless of whether the report was filed in the same accounting period in which the cause of action arose. The court held that the act did not violate the supremacy clause.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed, as modified, the judgment of the appellate court. The court concluded that to preserve the constitutionality of Corporation Business Activities Reporting Act, the statute was to be interpreted to mean that a foreign corporation could pursue a cause of action in the state after filing an Notice of Business Activities Report, regardless of whether the report was filed in the same accounting period in which the action arose.

 

208 of 2470 DOCUMENTS


Kimmelman v. Henkels & McCoy, Inc.


No. A-110


Supreme Court of New Jersey


108 N.J. 123; 527 A.2d 1368; 1987 N.J. LEXIS 351; 1987-2 Trade Cas. (CCH) P67,674


February 2, 1987, Argued  
July 22, 1987, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] On certification to the Superior Court, Appellate Division, whose opinion is reported at 208 N.J. Super. 508 (1986).
Kimmelman v. Henkels & McCoy, Inc., 208 N.J. Super. 508, 506 A.2d 381, 1986 N.J. Super. LEXIS 1189 (N.J. Super. Ct., 1986)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff New Jersey Attorney General appealed a decision of the Superior Court, Appellate Division (New Jersey), which affirmed the trial court's imposition of a lump sum civil penalty, rather than a per diem penalty, on defendants who violated the New Jersey Antitrust Act, N.J. Stat. Ann. §§ 56:9-1 to -19.


OVERVIEW: The New Jersey Attorney General filed a civil complaint against defendants, construction companies, for alleged violations of the New Jersey Antitrust Act, N.J. Stat. Ann. §§ 56:9-1 to -19. The trial court granted summary judgment to the Attorney General. The Attorney General asked that civil penalties of $ 2.1 million be imposed, which represented $ 500 for each day the conspiracy continued. The trial court instead imposed lump sum penalties of $ 100,000 or less, the appellate court affirmed, and the Attorney General appealed. The court found that the New Jersey Legislature intended to authorize the courts to impose per diem penalties that in the aggregate could exceed $ 100,000. The court identified several factors the New Jersey courts should consider in setting penalties, including the good or bad faith of defendant, defendant's ability to pay, amount of profits obtained from illegal activity, and injury to the public. The court reversed the appellate court's judgment as to the imposition of penalties and remanded the case to the trial court to determine the penalties.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the appellate court as to the imposition of civil penalties and remanded the case to the trial court to determine the appropriate penalties after finding that the New Jersey Antitrust Act authorized per diem penalties that in the aggregate exceeded $ 100,000.

 

209 of 2470 DOCUMENTS


State v. Dunbar


No. A-136


Supreme Court of New Jersey


108 N.J. 80; 527 A.2d 1346; 1987 N.J. LEXIS 343


March 16, 1987, Argued  
July 7, 1987, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] On certification to the Appellate DIVISION, Superior Court.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The state sought review of the judgment of the Superior Court (New Jersey), which eliminated a seven-year period of parole ineligibility from the trial court's sentence of defendant following his conviction for second-degree robbery in violation of N.J. Stat. Ann. § 2C:15-1.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was convicted of second-degree robbery, in violation of N.J. Stat. Ann. § 2C:15-1. Because he met the criteria for an extended term of imprisonment as a persistent offender under N.J. Stat. Ann. § 2C:43-7, defendant was exposed to a term of imprisonment between 10 and 20 years, with a presumptive term of 15 years. The trial court imposed the presumptive sentence of 15 years for a persistent offender convicted of second-degree crime and added a period of seven years of parole ineligibility. The appellate court agreed that defendant was eligible for sentencing as a persistent offender, but it found the imposition of a seven-year period of parole ineligibility on a 22-year old shocking and eliminated the period of ineligibility. In reversing the judgment of the appellate court and reinstating the trial court's sentence, the court held that given the totality of the circumstances (i.e., defendant's failure to respond to prior non-custodial efforts at rehabilitation and his conduct that was escalating in violence), the trial court's decision to impose an extended term with a period of parole ineligibility was made in essential accord with applicable sentencing guidelines.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the judgment of the appellate court eliminating defendant's period of ineligibility for parole and reinstated the sentence imposed by the trial court because it found that the trial court's decision to impose an extended term with a parole ineligibility period did not constitute such a clear error in judgment as to have warranted appellate revision.

 

210 of 2470 DOCUMENTS


State v. Weeks


No. A-106


Supreme Court of New Jersey


107 N.J. 396; 526 A.2d 1077; 1987 N.J. LEXIS 323


March 3, 1987, Argued  
June 25, 1987, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] On certification to the Appellate Division, Superior Court.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant sought review of a decision of the Appellate Division, Superior Court (New Jersey), which convicted him of first degree robbery, N.J. Stat. Ann. § 2C:15-1b, and sentenced him under the Graves Act, N.J. Stat. Ann. § 2C:43-6, following his conviction as an accomplice to robbery.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was charged in the lower court and convicted of first degree robbery and as an accomplice to first degree robbery. Defendant was sentenced pursuant to the Graves Act, , N.J. Stat. Ann. § 2C:43-6, and he appealed, claiming that such sentence violated his due process rights because the issue of eligibility for the mandatory sentence was not submitted separately to the jury. The court reversed, holding that the lower court erred in its instruction, which failed to convey to the jury that in order to convict the defendant as an accomplice to robbery in the first degree, it had to find that the defendant had the purpose to participate in that crime as defined under N.J. Stat. Ann. § 2C:15-1b. The court further held that the excessive cross-examination of the defendant concerning his knowledge of other alleged crimes of the principal was erroneous as well. Therefore, the court held that such errors required the reversal of defendant's conviction in the lower court and it remanded the case with orders to try defendant with correct instructions on the degree of his liability as an accomplice.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the lower court's judgment of defendant's conviction as an accomplice to robbery in the first degree, because of the erroneous instruction on the necessity for shared purpose to commit the underlying crime of first degree robbery, and because of an impermissibly broad introduction of evidence of other criminal conduct by the principal, which combined to taint the defendant's conviction.

 

211 of 2470 DOCUMENTS


Barone v. Dep't of Human Servs., Div. of Medical Assistance & Health Servs., Etc


No. A-85


Supreme Court of New Jersey


107 N.J. 355; 526 A.2d 1055; 1987 N.J. LEXIS 324


November 18, 1986, Argued  
June 23, 1987, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] On certification to the Superior Court, Appellate Division, whose opinion is reported at 210 N.J. Super. 276 (1985).
Barone v. Department of Human Services, Div. of Medical Assistance & Health Services, etc., 210 N.J. Super. 276, 509 A.2d 786, 1986 N.J. Super. LEXIS 1250 (App.Div., 1986)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant disabled persons challenged the judgment of the Superior Court, Appellate Division (New Jersey), which affirmed the decision of the Division of Medical Assistance and Health Services to deny appellants' benefit applications and found the New Jersey Pharmaceutical Assistance to the Aged and Disabled Act, N.J. Stat. Ann. § 30:4D-20 to -35, did not violate the equal protection clauses of the state and federal constitutions.


OVERVIEW: Appellant disabled persons were denied benefits under the New Jersey Pharmaceutical Assistance to the Aged and Disabled Act (PAAD), N.J. Stat. Ann. § 30: 4D-20 to -35, because appellants did not receive SSDI benefits. Appellants argued that the statute violated the equal protection clauses because it irrationally discriminated between those financially eligible disabled persons who received SSDI benefits and those who did not. The intermediate appellate court affirmed the judgment, as did the court, because it withstood scrutiny under both the state and federal constitutions. The classification was sustained under U.S. Const. amend. XIV because it was rationally related to a legitimate governmental purpose. It passed scrutiny under the state constitution because there was an appropriate governmental interest suitably furthered by the differential treatment. The purpose was to minimize the program's costs while maximizing its benefits to recipients, and the classification was rational, even though it was not the wisest, because it furthered the goal by eliminating the administrative costs that would have been incurred if the state were to make independent disability determinations.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment, which upheld the constitutionality of the New Jersey Pharmaceutical Assistance to the Aged and Disabled Act (PAAD), because the legislature's method of making the actual receipt of SSDI benefits a prerequisite to the receipt of PAAD benefits was rational and furthered the legitimate governmental purpose of minimizing the program's costs while maximizing its benefits to recipients.

 

212 of 2470 DOCUMENTS


Medici v. BPR Co.


No. A-54


Supreme Court of New Jersey


107 N.J. 1; 526 A.2d 109; 1987 N.J. LEXIS 309


October 20, 1986, Argued  
June 3, 1987, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] On Certification the Superior Court, Appellate Division.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant zoning applicant was approved by the board of adjustment for a variance to construct a motel. Plaintiff local property owner appealed to the governing body, and then to the Superior Court (New Jersey). Plaintiff settled and appellant property company intervened. The superior court denied the variance, and the appellate division reversed. Appellant challenged the order which reinstated the variance.


OVERVIEW: Defendant zoning applicant was approved by the board of adjustment (board) for a variance to construct a motel. Defendant had the burden to prove that the grant of the use variance for a motel at the intended site was not inconsistent with the intent and purpose of the zoning ordinance, and that the subject property was particularly suitable for the proposed use. No such proof was offered. No such finding was made by the board. Plaintiff local property owner appealed to the governing body, and then to the superior court. Plaintiff settled with defendant, and appellant property company intervened. The superior court denied the variance, and the appellate division reversed. Appellant challenged the order which reinstated the variance. The court reversed because defendant failed under his burden of proof. The court remanded to the board to permit defendant to attempt to supplement the record before the board, and to permit the board to adopt new findings and conclusions. The court held that the effect and application of its decision, except as to this case, was to be prospective only.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the judgment of the appellate division which had reinstated the variance because the record was inadequate to have permitted the variance. The court remanded back to the board of adjustment to permit defendant zoning applicant, if it desired, to attempt to supplement the record before the board, and to permit the board to adopt new findings and conclusions to support a variance.

 

213 of 2470 DOCUMENTS


New York State Dep't of Taxation & Finance v. Merritt, Inc.


No. A-118


Supreme Court of New Jersey


105 N.J. 584; 523 A.2d 654; 1987 N.J. LEXIS 297


February 17, 1987, Argued  
April 13, 1987, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] On certification to the Superior Court, Appellate Division.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant taxpayer, an officer in a New Jersey corporation doing business in New York, sought review of a judgment of the Superior Court, Appellate Division (New Jersey), which affirmed a trial court judgment against him in favor of appellee, department of taxation of New York. The New York judgment had been entered by issuance of an administrative warrant under N.Y. Tax Law § 1141(b). Appellant claimed he had received no notice of the judgment.


OVERVIEW: Appellee department of taxation of New York filed an action against appellant taxpayer, officer of a New Jersey corporation doing business in New York, for enforcement of a judgment against appellant for New York taxes due. Appellant contended that he had no notice or opportunity to be heard prior to entry of the personal judgment against him in New York. The trial court found in favor of appellee, and concluded that a determination of unconstitutionality could be made only by the courts of New York. The judgment was affirmed on appeal. On further appeal, the court reversed the lower courts' judgments, finding that it was unclear whether appellant had ever been granted notice and an opportunity to be heard, which were the only conditions under which the foreign judgment should have been honored. Appellee asserted that it had provided notice, and appellant denied receiving notice. The court concluded that it was unable to resolve whether notice was actually mailed or received and whether the address was one reasonably calculated to afford notice to appellant. Therefore, the court remanded the case for further proceedings on the issues it had raised.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the judgment of the appellate division, finding that there was not sufficient evidence on the record to determine whether appellant taxpayer had been provided with adequate notice and an opportunity to be heard before the foreign judgment was entered against him. Thus, the court remanded the matter to the trial court for further proceedings.

 

214 of 2470 DOCUMENTS


New Jersey State League of Municipalities v. Kimmelman


No. A-12


Supreme Court of New Jersey


105 N.J. 422; 522 A.2d 430; 1987 N.J. LEXIS 286


September 8, 1986, Argued  
March 26, 1987, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] On certification to the Superior Court, Appellate Division, whose opinion is reported at 204 N.J. Super. 323 (1985).


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellants, nonprofit organization and individuals, sought review of the order of the Superior Court, Appellate Division (New Jersey), which reversed the trial court's decision in favor of appellants in an action brought by appellants against appellee attorney general, seeking to declare that N.J. Stat. Ann. § 54:4-23(a) constituted a special law in violation of N.J. Const. art. IV, § 7, para. 9 and N.J. Const. art. VIII, § 1, para. 2.


OVERVIEW: Appellants, nonprofit organization and individuals, brought an action against appellee attorney general, seeking to declare that N.J. Stat. Ann. § 54:4-23(a), which exempted newly constructed unoccupied single family property from local property taxes, was a special law in violation of N.J. Const. art. IV, § 7, para. 9 and N.J. Const. art. VIII, § 1, para. 2. The trial court rendered judgment in favor of appellants on the ground that the statute was unconstitutional. The appeals court reversed, holding that the statute was general legislation in conformity with constitutional provisions. Based on its review of the history of the constitutional amendment, the court held that the property tax exemption was not consistent with the intent of the framers of N.J. Const. art. VIII, § 1, which was to preserve the statutory exemptions of property used for religious, educational, and religious purposes. The court held that the uniformity provision of N.J. Const. art. VIII, § 1, para.1 precluded the granting of the exemption at issue from the general burden of taxation. The court reversed the judgment of the appeals court.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the judgment of the appeals court reversing the decision in favor of appellants, nonprofit organization and individual, because the property tax exemption at issue violated the uniformity provision of the state constitution, which required that all real property be taxed by general law and uniform rules.

 

215 of 2470 DOCUMENTS


Zilinsky v. Zoning Bd. of Adjustment


No. A-79


Supreme Court of New Jersey


105 N.J. 363; 521 A.2d 841; 1987 N.J. LEXIS 280; 71 A.L.R.4th 519


November 17, 1986, Argued  
March 12, 1987, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] On certification to the Superior Court, Appellate Division.
Zilinsky v. Zoning Bd. of Adjustment, 104 N.J. 466, 517 A.2d 449, 1986 N.J. LEXIS 1834 (1986)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellants, zoning board and municipality, challenged the order of the Superior Court, Appellate Division (New Jersey), directing appellants to issue a certificate of occupancy for the conversion of a garage to a recreation room that respondent residential homeowner had undertaken without first obtaining a variance and building permit.


OVERVIEW: Respondent residential homeowner converted the attached garage of his residence into a recreation room, preventing the storage of a car. After the conversion, respondent applied to appellants, zoning board and municipality, for a variance after the building inspector granted a certificate of occupancy conditioned on obtaining a variance. Appellants denied the variance because respondent had failed to prove sufficient hardship. Respondent filed suit against appellants. The trial court found in favor of respondent, holding that the applicable zoning ordinance was unconstitutional. The intermediate court affirmed the trial court's judgment. The court reversed and held that the provision for an on-site garage space as a structural requirement in this area of the community was a valid exercise of the zoning power. The court ruled that the lower courts erred in concluding that the ordinance did not reasonably advance any of its purposes because the ordinance did not mandate use of the garage for car storage. The court found that the rational relationship between the means chosen to achieve the legitimate zoning purpose was at least debatable, and the ordinance was, therefore, sustained.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the order directing appellants, zoning board and municipality, to issue a certificate of occupancy to respondent residential homeowner, because the zoning ordinance at issue was constitutional as a valid exercise of the zoning power.

 

216 of 2470 DOCUMENTS


State v. Biegenwald


No. A-94-84


Supreme Court of New Jersey


106 N.J. 13; 524 A.2d 130; 1987 N.J. LEXIS 287


February 5, 1985, Argued  
March 5, 1987, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Later proceeding at State v. Biegenwald, 107 N.J. 649, 527 A.2d 469, 1987 N.J. LEXIS 395 (1987)
Petition granted by State v. Biegenwald, 110 N.J. 191, 540 A.2d 185, 1988 N.J. LEXIS 1725 (1988)
Appeal after remand at State v. Biegenwald, 110 N.J. 521, 542 A.2d 442, 1988 N.J. LEXIS 53 (1988)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] On appeal from the Superior Court, Law Division, Monmouth County.
State v. Ramseur, 106 N.J. 123, 524 A.2d 188, 1987 N.J. LEXIS 293 (1987)
State v. Biegenwald, 96 N.J. 630, 477 A.2d 318, 1984 N.J. LEXIS 2710 (1984)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed from the judgment of the Superior Court, Monmouth County (New Jersey), which convicted him, inter alia, of murder, N.J. Stat. Ann. § 2C:11-3a, and sentenced him to death pursuant to N.J. Stat. Ann. § 2C:11-3.


OVERVIEW: Defendant appealed his conviction of murder, N.J. Stat. Ann. § 2C:11-3a, and death sentence imposed pursuant to N.J. Stat. Ann. § 2C:11-3. The court affirmed the conviction but reversed the sentence and remanded for resentencing. The court held that the denial of defendant's motions for change of venue were not in error as the pre-trial publicity, though pervasive, did not result in an unfair or partial jury. However, the court held that the verbatim N.J. Stat. Ann. § 2C:11-3c(4)(c) charge on aggravating circumstances failed to channel the jury's discretion and was impermissibly vague. Additionally, the court sua sponte determined that the charge constituted plain error as it failed to provide that the state must prove beyond a reasonable doubt that the aggravating factors outweighed the mitigating ones. For purposes of retrial, resentencing could not be considered double jeopardy as the first sentence was for death and the evidence was sufficient, but the state could not charge any aggravating circumstances that were not found in the first sentencing phase. A new jury could be impaneled, and defendant could rely on the transcript, so long as the state was allowed to rebut.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed defendant's conviction of murder but reversed the death sentence and remanded for resentencing, because the trial court's verbatim statutory aggravating circumstances charge did not instruct the jury that the state had the burden of proving that the aggravating circumstances must outweigh the mitigating circumstances beyond a reasonable doubt.

 

217 of 2470 DOCUMENTS


State v. Ramseur


No. A-2


SUPREME COURT OF NEW JERSEY


106 N.J. 123; 524 A.2d 188; 1987 N.J. LEXIS 293


February 4, 1985, Argued  
March 5, 1987, Decided


NOTICE:

 [***1] [EDITOR'S NOTE: PART 1 OF 6. THIS DOCUMENT HAS BEEN SPLIT INTO MULTIPLE PARTS ON LEXIS TO ACCOMMODATE ITS LARGE SIZE. EACH PART CONTAINS THE SAME LEXIS CITE.]


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Companion case at State v. Biegenwald, 106 N.J. 13, 524 A.2d 130, 1987 N.J. LEXIS 287 (1987)
Habeas corpus proceeding at Ramseur v. Beyer, 921 F.2d 504, 1990 U.S. App. LEXIS 22253 (3d Cir. N.J., 1990)


PRIOR HISTORY: On appeal from the Superior Court, Law Division, Essex County.
State v. Ramseur, 197 N.J. Super. 565, 485 A.2d 708, 1984 N.J. Super. LEXIS 1247 (Law Div., 1984)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant sought review of the judgment of the New Jersey Superior Court, Law Division, which convicted defendant of murder and imposed a sentence of death.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was convicted of murder and sentenced to death under the Capital Punishment Act (Act), 1982 N.J. Laws c. 111. Defendant contended that the Act was unconstitutional under the federal and state constitutions. Defendant also contended that various trial errors warranted reversal of defendant's conviction and sentence. The court affirmed defendant's conviction but reversed his sentence and directed that, on remand, the death penalty could not be imposed on defendant and that defendant was to be sentenced to imprisonment for a specific term of years with no parole eligibility for 30 years. The court held that the Act was constitutional in all respects under the federal and state constitutions. However, the court held that the trial court gave impermissible supplemental instructions to the jury during the sentencing phase that coerced the jury to reach a unanimous verdict as the instructions incorrectly suggested different kinds of adverse consequences that would have been caused by a hung jury. The court held that the supplemental instructions improperly relieved the jury of full responsibility for the death decision.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed defendant's conviction but reversed his sentence. The court held that the Capital Punishment Act was constitutional. The court reversed the sentence because the jury was improperly relieved of the full responsibility for the death decision as a result of coercive supplemental jury instructions.

221 of 2470 DOCUMENTS


State v. Ramseur


No. A-2


SUPREME COURT OF NEW JERSEY


106 N.J. 123; 524 A.2d 188; 1987 N.J. LEXIS 293


February 4, 1985, Argued  
March 5, 1987, Decided


NOTICE:

[EDITOR'S NOTE: PART 6 OF 6. THIS DOCUMENT HAS BEEN SPLIT INTO MULTIPLE PARTS ON LEXIS TO ACCOMMODATE ITS LARGE SIZE. EACH PART CONTAINS THE SAME LEXIS CITE.]


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Companion case at State v. Biegenwald, 106 N.J. 13, 524 A.2d 130, 1987 N.J. LEXIS 287 (1987)
Habeas corpus proceeding at Ramseur v. Beyer, 921 F.2d 504, 1990 U.S. App. LEXIS 22253 (3d Cir. N.J., 1990)


PRIOR HISTORY: On appeal from the Superior Court, Law Division, Essex County.
State v. Ramseur, 197 N.J. Super. 565, 485 A.2d 708, 1984 N.J. Super. LEXIS 1247 (Law Div., 1984)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed from a judgment of the Superior Court, Law Division, Essex County (New Jersey), which convicted him of murder and imposed a sentence of death. Defendant attacked the constitutionality of the New Jersey Capital Punishment Act, 1982 N.J. Laws c. 111, under the federal and New Jersey Constitutions. He also contended that various trial errors warranted the reversal of his conviction and sentence.


OVERVIEW: The jury found defendant guilty of murdering a woman. During the sentencing proceedings, the jury sent a note to the trial court stating that it was unable to reach a unanimous decision. The trial court charged the jurors to continue deliberating and emphasized the importance of reaching a unanimous verdict. Eventually the jury found that two aggravating factors, the vileness of the murder and defendant's prior murder conviction, outweighed the mitigating factors of defendant's mental stress and mental defect, and sentenced him to death. On appeal, the court upheld the constitutionality of the New Jersey Capital Punishment Act and affirmed defendant's conviction. However, the court reversed defendant's death sentence and directed that, on remand, defendant was to be sentenced to a prison term. The court found that the trial court gave improper supplemental charges that undoubtedly coerced the jurors to conclude that a unanimous verdict was their only acceptable alternative. The court held that the trial court committed a reversible error by failing to inform the jury of the statutory option of returning a final, non-unanimous verdict that would result in defendant's imprisonment.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed defendant's conviction but reversed his sentence. The court held that the Capital Punishment Act was constitutional. The court reversed the sentence because the jury was improperly relieved of the full responsibility for the death decision as a result of coercive supplemental jury instructions.

223 of 2470 DOCUMENTS


State v. Valentin


No. A-42


Supreme Court of New Jersey


105 N.J. 14; 519 A.2d 322; 1987 N.J. LEXIS 266


September 22, 1986, Argued  
January 13, 1987, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] On appeal from the Superior Court, Appellate Division, whose opinion is reported at 208 N.J. Super. 536 (1986).


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The state appealed the judgment of the New Jersey Superior Court, Appellate Division, which held that N.J. Stat. Ann. § 2C-29-3b(4) was not applicable where defendant was stopped for a routine traffic violation and, in response to a request by the police officer for his name, gave a false name.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was stopped twice during the same day by the same police officer for routine traffic offenses. On both occasions, he was asked his name. During the initial stop, defendant gave a false name, but, on the second stop, he gave his correct name. Recognizing the discrepancy, the officer charged defendant with a violation of N.J. Stat. Ann. § 2C:39-3b(4), which made it a crime to volunteer false information to a law enforcement officer. The trial court dismissed the false information charge because it found that an individual who responded to a question from an officer had not "volunteered" that information. Thus, a requirement of the statute was not met. The lower court affirmed the judgment. On appeal, the state argued that the lower court erred in its construction of the statute. In affirming the judgment, the court found that penal statutes were strictly construed and must be sufficiently definite to advise ordinary people of the conduct which they prohibited. Given the plain language of the statute, its legislative history, and its similarity to another statute, the court found that it was not applicable to the circumstances in this case.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the dismissal of the false information charge against defendant because, based on the plain language and legislative history of the controlling statute, the state could not have prosecuted defendant for volunteering false information where he gave a false name in response to a question from the police at a routine traffic stop.

 

224 of 2470 DOCUMENTS


State v. Novembrino


No. A-76


Supreme Court of New Jersey


105 N.J. 95; 519 A.2d 820; 1987 N.J. LEXIS 265


February 19, 1986, Argued  
January 7, 1987, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] On appeal from the Superior Court, Appellate Division, whose opinion is reported at 200 N.J. Super. 229 (1985).
State v. Novembrino, 200 N.J. Super. 229, 491 A.2d 37, 1985 N.J. Super. LEXIS 1695 (App.Div., 1985)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The state appealed from the judgment of the Superior Court, Appellate Division (New Jersey), which affirmed the trial court's grant of defendant's N.J. Ct. R. 3:5-7 motion to suppress evidence on the ground that the police officer's affidavit supporting the search warrant for defendant's gas station did not establish probable cause.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was indicted for possession of controlled substances and possession with intent to distribute after his property was searched and drugs were discovered. A search warrant had been obtained based on an informant's conclusory, non-specific observations and on a police officer's statement that, afterwards, he saw defendant engage in what appeared to be a drug transaction. Defendant's motion to suppress was granted. The lower court affirmed, from which the state appealed. The court affirmed, holding that New Jersey did not recognize the good faith exception to the exclusionary rule. Federal courts recognized the rule that evidence seized pursuant to a warrant issued without probable cause need not be excluded if the police officer who executed the warrant objectively relied in good faith on the defective warrant in gathering evidence. The court held that N.J. Const. art. I, § 7, guaranteed New Jersey citizens protection from unreasonable search and seizures, that uniformity between federal and state courts was not essential, and that the exception would erode the effective procedures employed by the criminal justice system to accommodate the constitutional guarantee.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment affirming the trial court's grant of defendant's motion to suppress evidence, as the warrant was defective and there was no good faith exception to the state constitutional exclusionary rule.

 

225 of 2470 DOCUMENTS


In re Petition of Felmeister & Isaacs


No. A-95


Supreme Court of New Jersey


104 N.J. 515; 518 A.2d 188; 1986 N.J. LEXIS 1258


October 22, 1984, Argued  
December 10, 1986, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] On petition for review of Disciplinary Rule 2-102(A).


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner law firm sought review of N.J. Ct. R., Code Prof. Conduct DR 7.2(a), which regulated attorney advertising by prohibiting the use of drawings, animations, dramatizations, music, or lyrics, and required that all advertisements be presented in a dignified manner.


OVERVIEW: Petitioner law firm sought review of N.J. Ct. R., Code Prof. Conduct DR 7.2(a), contending that it violated the U.S. Const. amend. I right to freedom of speech. Rule 7.2(a) prohibited attorney advertisements from using drawings, animations, dramatizations, music, or lyrics, and required all advertisements to be presented in a dignified manner. The court revised Rule 7.2(a) by limiting the specific restrictions to television advertisements and eliminating the dignified presentation requirement. The court held that the new requirement that an advertisement be predominantly informational served the substantial state interest of assuring that consumer decisions in selecting counsel were reasonable. The television restrictions served to prevent an undue irrational influence on consumers. The absolute restriction on extreme portrayals served the interest of preserving public confidence. Finally, the court held that the restrictions directly served these interests and were the least restrictive alternatives necessary to achieve these results. The Supreme Court Committee on Attorney Advertising was formed to determine compliance, conduct and report on studies, and issue advisory opinions.


OUTCOME: The court, while not expressly granting petitioner's request to review the constitutionality of its restrictions on attorney advertising, modified its rule to require that all advertisements be predominantly informational, retained the prohibition on the use of drawings, animations, dramatizations, music, or lyrics in television advertisements, and prohibited extreme portrayals. The dignified portrayal requirement was eliminated.

 

226 of 2470 DOCUMENTS


State v. Ragland


No. A-115


Supreme Court of New Jersey


105 N.J. 189; 519 A.2d 1361; 1986 N.J. LEXIS 1259


February 19, 1986, Argued  
November 21, 1986, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] On a rehearing of the judgment of this Court reported at 101 N.J. 33 (1985).


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: On rehearing, the state challenged the judgment of the Supreme Court of New Jersey, which reversed defendant's conviction for possession of a weapon by a convicted felon under N. J. Stat. Ann. § 2C: 39-7.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was convicted of conspiracy to commit armed robbery, unlawful possession of a weapon, and unlawful possession of a weapon without a permit. Another charge against him, possession of a weapon by a convicted felon, was severed on defense counsel's motion. After the jury's guilty verdict, the severed charge was tried before the same jury. That jury had just convicted defendant of charges that necessarily included a finding that defendant was in "possession of a weapon." The jury returned a guilty verdict, and defendant appealed, alleging that the jury instruction deprived him of his right to a fair trial by jury, because it amounted to an instruction to the jury not to consider independently the question of possession, but rather to abide by its prior determination of that fact. The appellate court affirmed, and did so again after the court reversed its initial affirmation of the conviction. Defendant again appealed. The court affirmed its previous judgment that reversed defendant's conviction for possession of a weapon by a convicted felon, and the court held that the jury charge had the unavoidable effect of directing a guilty verdict.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed its previous judgment, which reversed defendant's conviction for possession of a weapon by a convicted felon, because the jury charge that was given to the same jury that had just previously found defendant guilty of an offense where they had to find that defendant was in possession of a weapon in order to convict had the unavoidable effect of directing a guilty verdict against defendant.

 

227 of 2470 DOCUMENTS


State v. Whitehead


No. A-103


Supreme Court of New Jersey


104 N.J. 353; 517 A.2d 373; 1986 N.J. LEXIS 1247


March 17, 1986, Argued  
November 19, 1986, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] On certification to the Superior Court, Appellate Division


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed from a judgment of the Superior Court (New Jersey), which affirmed defendant's conviction for aggravated sexual assault and held that the trial court's ruling was not reviewable on appeal because defendant did not testify at trial after the trial court determined that defendant's prior convictions could be used to impeach him if he testified.


OVERVIEW: At defendant's trial for aggravated sexual assault and possession of a weapon with an unlawful purpose, defendant requested a hearing to determine whether two prior convictions could be used on cross-examination to impeach his credibility. After conducting a hearing, the trial court ruled that the convictions could be so used. Defendant declined to testify and was convicted of aggravated sexual assault. The superior court affirmed the conviction, ruling that because defendant did not testify, the trial court's ruling was not reviewable on appeal. On appeal, the court reversed, holding that defendant did not have to testify at trial to obtain appellate review of the trial court's ruling that defendant's convictions could be used for impeachment purposes. The court found that defendant's testimony was unnecessary for a trial court to make the discretionary determination on the admissibility of prior convictions and for an appellate court to review the trial court's exercise of discretion. The court also found that defendant was not required to make an advance offer of proof because significant problems could arise by requiring defendant to describe his testimony before trial.


OUTCOME: The court reversed because defendant did not have to testify at trial to obtain appellate review of a trial court's ruling that defendant's convictions could be used for impeachment purposes as defendant's testimony was unnecessary for the trial court to make the discretionary determination on the admissibility of prior convictions and for an appellate court to review the trial court's exercise of discretion.

 

228 of 2470 DOCUMENTS


In re Promulgation of Guardianship Servs. Regulations


No. A-140


Supreme Court of New Jersey


103 N.J. 619; 512 A.2d 453; 1986 N.J. LEXIS 1224


March 17, 1986, Argued  
July 30, 1986, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] On certification to the Superior Court, Appellate Division, whose opinion is reported at 198 N.J. Super. 132 (1984).


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant public advocate, challenged the judgment of the Superior Court (New Jersey), which upheld respondent Department of Human Services, Division of Developmental Disabilities' regulations regarding the guardianship of mentally retarded minors whose parents were absent and unable to be located.


OVERVIEW: Appellant public advocate filed an action against respondent Department of Human Services, Division of Developmental Disabilities, challenging respondent's rules regarding the guardianship of mentally retarded minors whose parents were absent and unable to be located. Appellant alleged that the regulations exceeded respondent's authority, unconstitutionally discriminated against mentally retarded minors, and violated the due process rights of the affected parents. The appellate court upheld the regulations. On appeal, the judgment of the appellate court was affirmed, and the court held that the regulations were clearly within the fair contemplation of the delegation of the enabling statute, that the regulations rationally furthered an important state interest and therefore comported with equal protection, and that due process did not require a judicial hearing in the circumstances contemplated by the regulations.


OUTCOME: The judgment of the appellate court was affirmed because the regulations of respondent Department of Human Services, Division of Developmental Disabilities were clearly within the fair contemplation of the enabling statute, the regulations rationally furthered an important state interest and therefore comported with equal protection, and due process did not require a judicial hearing in the circumstances contemplated by the regulations.

 

229 of 2470 DOCUMENTS


Mack Paramus Co. v. Paramus


No. A-101


Supreme Court of New Jersey


103 N.J. 564; 511 A.2d 1179; 1986 N.J. LEXIS 976


March 3, 1986, Argued  
July 23, 1986, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Later proceeding at Mack Paramus Co. v. Paramus, 228 N.J. Super. 234, 549 A.2d 474, 1988 N.J. Super. LEXIS 392 (N.J. Super. Ct., 1988)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] On certification to the Superior Court, Appellate Division, whose opinion is reported at 201 N.J. Super. 508 (1985).
Mack Paramus Co. v. Paramus, 201 N.J. Super. 508, 493 A.2d 593, 1985 N.J. Super. LEXIS 1325 (N.J. Super. Ct., 1985)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant municipalities sought review of the order of the Superior Court, Appellate Division, which held that local blue laws were invalid, as foreclosed by the state blue law, under N.J. Stat. Ann. §§2A:171-5.8to -5.18, in a suit brought by respondent businesses found guilty of violating the local ordinances.


OVERVIEW: In two originally separate actions, respondent businesses were found guilty of violating local ordinances, enacted by appellant municipalities, to require stricter blue laws than were required under the state's blue law, N.J. Stat. Ann. §§ 2A:171-5.8 to -5.18 (1959), under chapter 119 of the New Jersey Code of Criminal Justice, N.J. Stat. Ann. §§ 2C:1-1 to :98-4. In appealing the guilty verdicts, respondents raised the question of whether the state blue law foreclosed the power of a municipality to enact local blue laws. The appellant division ruled the local blue laws were invalid and appellants sought review. In the consolidated action, the court reversed the order of the appellate division, holding that the state law did not preempt the power of municipalities to enact blue law ordinances, which were not in conflict with the state law. As there was legislative intent that municipalities ordain their own blue laws, there was no basis for invalidating appellants' ordinances.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the appellate division's order, invalidating local blue law ordinances enacted by appellant municipalities, holding that the state law did not preempt the power of municipalities to enact blue law ordinances, which were not in conflict with the state law. Therefore, respondent businesses' contention, that the more general state law preempted the power of the municipality to enact more restrictive ordinances, was incorrect.

 

230 of 2470 DOCUMENTS


State v. Gilmore


No. A-53


Supreme Court of New Jersey


103 N.J. 508; 511 A.2d 1150; 1986 N.J. LEXIS 970


February 19, 1986, Argued  
July 16, 1986, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] On certification to the Superior Court, Appellate Division, whose opinion is reported at 199 N.J. Super. 389 (1985).
State v. Gilmore, 199 N.J. Super. 389, 489 A.2d 1175, 1985 N.J. Super. LEXIS 1402 (App.Div., 1985)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The State appealed a judgement of the Superior Court, Appellate Division (New Jersey), which reversed defendant's conviction for first degree robbery.


OVERVIEW: An all white jury convicted defendant, who was black, of three first-degree robberies. During jury selection, the prosecution excused all nine black potential jurors. The prosecutor stated he wanted black jurors who were of the professional type and without maternal family instincts. The prosecutor excused two female black jurors for these reasons, but did not excuse six white females, including five homemakers and one secretary. After jury selection, defense counsel made a motion for a mistrial, which was denied. On appeal, the convictions were reversed and a new trial was granted. The court granted certification and affirmed the judgment of the appeals court. The court held that the N.J. Const. art. I, paras. 5, 9, and 10 forbade a prosecutor from exercising peremptory challenges to remove potential petit jurors that were members of a cognizable group on the basis of their presumed group bias. The court determined the defendant had established that the prosecutor impermissibly excluded all black jurors.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the reversal of defendant's conviction for first degree robbery.

 

231 of 2470 DOCUMENTS


Edgewater Inv. Assocs. v. Edgewater


No. A-82


Supreme Court of New Jersey


103 N.J. 227; 510 A.2d 1178; 1986 N.J. LEXIS 964


February 3, 1986, Argued  
July 10, 1986, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] On appeal from the Superior Court, Appellate Division, whose opinion is reported at 201 N.J. Super. 267 (1985).


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant unit owner challenged the order of the Superior Court, Appellate Division (New Jersey), which held that the Senior Citizens and Disabled Protected Tenancy Act, N.J. Stat. Ann. § 2A:18-61.22 to 61.39 applied to respondent tenants retroactively.


OVERVIEW: Appellant unit owner purchased an apartment complex and began converting it into condominiums. Two months later, the Senior Citizens and Disabled Protected Tenancy Act, § 2A:18-61.22 to 61.39 (act), was enacted. The act was designed to protect elderly renters from the conversion of their homes. The trial court found that the act applied retroactively. Respondent tenants and appellant sought review. The reviewing court affirmed and appellant again sought review. On appeal, the court affirmed and found that the legislature's goal was to protect elderly tenants from eviction. The court found that the act's effect on appellant's contract rights under U.S. Const. art. I, § 10 and N.J. Const. art. IV, § VII, para. 3., was reasonable and was substantially related to a legitimate goal. The court upheld the retroactive portion of the act as also having a legitimate legislative purpose supported by rational means. The court further found that appellant had not suffered a manifest injustice due to the fact that the legislation was pending when appellant purchased the property. The court found that it was appropriate for respondents to come under the retroactive portions of the act.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the lower court's judgment, which found that respondent tenants were retroactively protected as tenants, because the public interest to protect elderly tenants sufficiently outweighed the private rights impaired and because the statute did not result in a manifest injustice to appellant unit owner.

 

232 of 2470 DOCUMENTS


State v. Hartley


No. A-93


Supreme Court of New Jersey


103 N.J. 252; 511 A.2d 80; 1986 N.J. LEXIS 1265


February 20, 1985, Argued  
July 3, 1986, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] On appeal from the Superior Court, Appellate Division.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed the convictions before the Superior Court, Appellate Division (New Jersey), for one count of first degree robbery and two counts of felony murder.


OVERVIEW: After serving as a lookout during a robbery in which two people were killed, defendant was convicted of one count of first degree robbery and two counts of felony murder. Defendant appealed, questioning the admissibility of his two inculpatory statements given during custodial interrogations by the Federal Bureau of Investigation (FBI), and state and municipal authorities. The court reversed the convictions and remanded the cause for retrial. Defendant had previously been given Miranda warnings and had asserted his right to remain silent. Later he made a statement in response to interrogation by federal authorities who did not give defendant new Miranda warnings. Defendant made a second statement to state and municipal authorities after defendant had been reinformed of his Miranda rights. The federal authorities' failure scrupulously to honor defendant's intent to remain silent required the exclusion of both statements. Though defendant had been given new Miranda warnings before making the second statement, it was obtained through a process that was part of the same illicit procedure that produced the first statement.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the convictions for first degree robbery and felony murder and remanded the matter for a new trial. The federal authorities' failure to scrupulously honor defendant's intent to remain silent required the exclusion on the state's case of defendant's confession to agents of the Federal Bureau of Investigation and of defendant's second statement to state and municipal authorities.

Continental Trailways, Inc. v. Director, Div. of Motor Vehicles


A-48


Supreme Court of New Jersey


102 N.J. 526; 509 A.2d 769; 1986 N.J. LEXIS 949


November 19, 1985, Argued  
May 27, 1986, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] On appeal from the Superior Court, Appellate Division, whose opinion is reported at 201 N.J. Super. 257 (1985).


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant, Director of the Division of Motor Vehicles, appealed as a matter of right from a decision of the Superior Court, Appellate Division (New Jersey), which held that the autobus excise tax imposed under N.J. Stat. Ann. § 48:4-20 violated the commerce clause of U.S. Const. art. 1, § 8, cl. 3 and ordered defendant to refund taxes paid by plaintiff common carrier.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiff, a common carrier providing intrastate and interstate bus service, applied for a refund of excise taxes and the tax court held that the tax imposed by N.J. Stat. Ann. § 48:4-20 unconstitutionally discriminated against interstate commerce and ordered defendant Director of the Division of Motor Vehicles to refund tax payments. The lower court affirmed and defendant appealed as a matter of right. The director claimed that the tax's intent was not to restrict interstate competition or promote economic protectionism, that interstate and intrastate carriers did not compete, and that the state interest in promoting local bus service outweighed any burden on interstate commerce. The court rejected defendant's claims and held that the tax discriminated against plaintiff and other interstate bus companies in violation of the commerce clause, U.S. Const. art. 1, § 8, cl. 3. Plaintiff was not entitled to a refund, however, because in the absence of statutory authority there could be no recovery of taxes voluntarily paid under a mistake of law. The lower court's order directing a refund was reversed, and the judgment as modified was affirmed.


OUTCOME: The court found that the tax discriminated against interstate commerce in violation of the commerce clause but there was no statutory authority for a refund of a tax voluntarily paid under a mistake of law. The court affirmed the judgment insofar as it held that the statute unconstitutionally discriminated against interstate commerce and reversed the decision insofar as it directed a refund of taxes. As modified, the judgment was affirmed.

Garfield Trust Co. v. Director, Division of Taxation


A-85


Supreme Court of New Jersey


102 N.J. 420; 508 A.2d 1104; 1986 N.J. LEXIS 945


January 22, 1986, Argued  
May 22, 1986, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Appeal dismissed by Garfield Trust Co. v. Director, Div. of Taxation, 479 U.S. 925, 107 S. Ct. 390, 93 L. Ed. 2d 345, 1986 U.S. LEXIS 4446 (1986)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] On certification to the Superior Court, Appellate Division.
Garfield Trust Co. v. Director, Div. of Taxation, 7 N.J. Tax 664, 1985 N.J. Tax LEXIS 31 (N.J. Super. Ct., 1985)
Garfield Trust Co. v. Director, Div. of Taxation, 102 N.J. 303, 508 A.2d 189, 1985 N.J. LEXIS 2965 (1985)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner commercial banking corporation appealed an order of the Superior Court, Appellate Division (New Jersey), which affirmed a judgment of the Tax Court, affirming a decision of respondent Director of the Division of Taxation to deny petitioner's refund claim.


OVERVIEW: Petitioner commercial banking corporation filed a complaint with the Tax Court after respondent Director of the Division of Taxation denied petitioner's refund claim. Petitioner had included the face value of federal, state, and local obligations in its net worth tax base, and included interest income from the securities in its net income tax base. After paying the tax, petitioner filed the refund claim. The Tax Court affirmed the Director's denial, the appellate division affirmed, and the court affirmed. The court held that inclusion of the face value and interest income of federal obligations in the bases for calculating New Jersey Corporation Business Tax (CBT) did not violate the Federal Public Debt Statute, 31 U.S.C.S. § 742, because the CBT was a nondiscriminatory annual franchise tax. The court concluded that § 742 did not exempt the principal and interest income of federal obligations from inclusion in the net worth and net income bases for computing the CBT, and that the state enabling legislation did not exempt the principal and interest income of state obligations from inclusion in these CBT bases.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment of the appellate division, holding that petitioner commercial banking corporation's refund claim was properly denied because inclusion of the face value and interest income of federal obligations in the bases for calculating the New Jersey Corporation Business Tax did not violate the Federal Public Debt Statute.

 

235 of 2470 DOCUMENTS



Hills Dev. Co. v. Bernards


Nos. A-122, A-123, A-124, A-125, A-126, A-127, A-128, A-129, A-130, A-131, A-132, A-133


Supreme Court of New Jersey


103 N.J. 1; 510 A.2d 621; 1986 N.J. LEXIS 868


January 6, 1986and January 7, 1986, Argued in Part and Submitted in Part  
February 20, 1986, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Costs and fees proceeding at, Remanded by Urban League of Greater New Brunswick v. Township Committee of Cranbury, 222 N.J. Super. 131, 536 A.2d 287, 1987 N.J. Super. LEXIS 1429 (App.Div., 1987)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] On certification to the Superior Court, Law and Chancery Divisions.
Southern Burlington County NAACP v. Mt. Laurel, 92 N.J. 158, 456 A.2d 390, 1983 N.J. LEXIS 2344 (1983)
Morris County Fair Housing Council v. Boonton Township, 209 N.J. Super. 393, 507 A.2d 768, 1985 N.J. Super. LEXIS 1651 (Law Div., 1985)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant townships challenged, on certification, the orders of various Superior Court, Law and Chancery Divisions (New Jersey) that largely refused to transfer pending and future litigation concerning the obligation to create of low and moderate income housing to the Council on Affordable Housing under the Fair Housing Act, N.J. Stat. Ann. § 52:27D-311 et seq., and contended that the Act was unconstitutional.


OVERVIEW: The Council on Affordable Housing was created in the Fair Housing Act, N.J. Stat. Ann. § 52:27D-311 et seq., in response to litigation on the constitutional obligation to create a realistic opportunity for the construction of low and moderate income housing. Appellant townships challenged the validity of the denial of various motions to transfer litigation to the Council under the Act. The court ordered all of the cases transferred, as the Act's purpose was to transfer all such cases, except where unforeseen and exceptional unfairness, not applicable here, would result. Further, the Act was constitutional. It did not delay the constitutional obligation as there was no such timetable implicit therein and it was designed to satisfy this obligation within a reasonable time period. The builder's remedy moratorium applied to litigation, not Council matters and was never part of the constitutional obligation. It was speculation to claim that the Act would not achieve the construction of lower income housing. The Act provided many incentives that the judicial remedy did not have. Finally, judicial review of ordinances was provided for under the Act.


OUTCOME: The court ordered that all of the cases concerning the constitutional obligation to create a realistic opportunity for the construction of low and moderate income housing be transferred to the Council on Affordable Housing because they fell under the Fair Housing Act and they did not involve unforeseen and exceptional unfairness. The Act was constitutional.

Riggs v. Long Beach


A-49 September Term 1985


Supreme Court of New Jersey


101 N.J. 515; 503 A.2d 284; 1986 N.J. LEXIS 863


October 21, 1985, Argued  
January 21, 1986, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: On remand at Riggs v. Township of Long Beach, 212 N.J. Super. 69, 514 A.2d 45, 1986 N.J. Super. LEXIS 1645 (App.Div., 1986)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] On certification to the Superior Court, Appellate Division.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff landowners brought suit against defendant township, challenging a zoning ordinance. The New Jersey Superior Court, Appellate Division, reversed the trial court's decision that the ordinance was arbitrary, capricious, and unconstitutional, finding that the ordinance had become moot because it was replaced by a new one and that plaintiffs had failed to exhaust their administrative remedies. Plaintiffs challenged the decision.


OVERVIEW: In a suit by plaintiff landowners against defendant township challenging a zoning ordinance, the lower court reversed the trial court's decision that the ordinance was arbitrary, capricious, and unconstitutional. The court reversed the lower court's decision, remanding the matter to the lower court for reconsideration. It held that the lower court erred in finding that the ordinance was moot under the time-of-decision rule because it had been replaced by a new ordinance, noting that the new ordinance did not change the substance of the previous one, but merely corrected a technical inadequacy. Therefore, it concluded, the lower court erred in remanding the case to the trial court for consideration under the new ordinance. It held that plaintiffs had not failed to exhaust their administrative remedies by not applying for a variance, writing that such a remedy was inappropriate when plaintiffs were challenging the constitutionality of the ordinance. Moreover, it held, because defendant alleged that the ordinance applied only to plaintiffs' property, it was appropriate for the lower court to address the legal question of whether it legitimately served the public interest.


OUTCOME: Finding that plaintiff landowners' challenge to the constitutionality of defendant township's ordinance was not rendered moot by a new ordinance and that plaintiffs were not required to apply for a variance before filing suit, the court reversed the lower court's order reversing the trial court's decision. It remanded the case to the lower court for its reconsideration.

 

237 of 2470 DOCUMENTS


State v. LeFurge


A-34


Supreme Court of New Jersey


101 N.J. 404; 502 A.2d 35; 1986 N.J. LEXIS 860


September 24, 1985, Argued  
January 9, 1986, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] On certification to the Superior Court, Appellate Division.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The prosecution challenged by certification to the Superior Court, Appellate Division (New Jersey), a decision affirming the trial court's order that arrested defendant's judgment of conviction and dismissed a conspiracy charge, pursuant to N.J. Stat. Ann. § 2C:1-8(d)(2), on the ground that the conviction could not stand because no overt act in furtherance of the conspiracy had been alleged in the indictment.


OVERVIEW: In issue was the constitutionality of N.J. Stat. Ann. § 2C:1-8(d)(2), which provided that a defendant might be convicted of a conspiracy to commit a substantive offense even though the crime of conspiracy was not charged in the indictment. Defendant had been indicted for theft. Pursuant to N.J. Stat. Ann. § 2C:1-8(d)(2), the trial court instructed the jury to consider, as an included offense, defendant's guilt of the crime of conspiracy to commit theft. Defendant was acquitted of theft and convicted of conspiracy. Following the conviction, on defendant's motion, the trial court arrested the judgment of conviction and dismissed the conspiracy charge. The trial court reasoned that the conviction could not stand because no overt act in furtherance of the conspiracy had been alleged in the indictment. The lower court affirmed. On appeal by the prosecution, the court found that neither an overt act nor the conspiracy itself was required to be alleged in the indictment. The court concluded that the application of § 2C:1-8(d)(2) in this case did not offend the constitutional guarantee of indictment by grand jury. The court reversed and reinstated the conspiracy conviction.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the trial court's order that arrested the judgment of conviction and dismissed the conspiracy charge, on the grounds that neither an overt act nor the conspiracy itself was required to be alleged in the indictment. The court reinstated defendant's conspiracy conviction.

In re Randolph


No. A-46


Supreme Court of New Jersey


101 N.J. 425; 502 A.2d 533; 1986 N.J. LEXIS 859


September 10, 1984, Argued  
January 7, 1986, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] On petition for review of Rule 1:17-1.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner, a court attendant, sought review of a decision by the assignment judge of Monmouth County (New Jersey), which ordered the petitioner to discontinue her service in political offices and her service as an advocate for private organizations.


OVERVIEW: Shortly after her employment commenced, the petitioner, a court attendant, submitted a list of her outside activities to the court administrator. After review, the administrative office of the courts advised the petitioner that she would be required to discontinue certain activities. The assignment judge entered an order of compliance. The court granted a petition for review and ordered a factual hearing as to the petition. The court affirmed the decision of the assignment judge and held that the petitioner's activities posed a realistic likelihood of involving her in important and recurring public issues that were frequently the subject of political controversy. Furthermore, the court found that the petitioner's activities gave the impression of judicial involvement in those issues. Lastly, the court held that the petitioner's activities created the appearance of a judiciary lacking in impartiality either in general or on such social or political issues. The court concluded that the petitioner's activities realistically posed the likelihood of a disruptive effect upon the work of the judiciary.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed, holding that the petitioner's activities realistically posed the likelihood of a disruptive effect upon the work of the judiciary. Accordingly, the court denied the petitioner's request to continue the actives, while she remained in the service of the court.
