Coons v. American Honda Motor Co.


A-87


Supreme Court of New Jersey


94 N.J. 307; 463 A.2d 921; 1983 N.J. LEXIS 2742


February 23, 1983, Argued  
August 3, 1983, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] On certification to the Superior Court, Appellate Division.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The cause was remanded by the United States Supreme Court to the court, which on its own motion certified the cause, to clarify an ambiguity in the court's past interpretation of N.J. Stat. Ann. § 2A:14-22, which tolled the running of the applicable statute of limitations in actions against foreign corporations that were not represented in the state, and to decide whether the statute violated the Commerce Clause, U.S. Const. art. 1, § 8, cl. 3.


OVERVIEW: On remand from the United States Supreme Court, the court had to determine the applicability and constitutionality of N.J. Stat. Ann. § 2A:14-22, which tolled statutes of limitation for parties who were not present in the state for service of process. Respondent filed an action against a corporation that had a certificate of authority to do business in the state, N.J. Stat. Ann. § 14A:13-4, and appellant foreign corporation that did not. The trial court granted the "native" corporation's motion for summary judgment, but denied that of appellant, ruling that the statute of limitations, N.J. Stat. Ann. § 2A:14-1, had been tolled by § 2A:14-22 because appellant was not represented in New Jersey by a person upon whom process could be served. The court held that a corporation must register under § 14A:13-4 before it could file a notice designating an agent for service of process, N.J. Stat. Ann. § 14A:1-6(4), and enjoy the benefit of § 2A:14-1. Therefore, the court concluded that § 2A:14-22 was a forced-licensure law that violated the Commerce Clause, U.S. Const. art. 1, § 8, cl. 3, reversed judgment for respondent, and remanded for entry of judgment for appellant.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the judgment and remanded for entry of judgment for appellant foreign corporation, because appellant was entitled to summary judgment on the affirmative defense of statute of limitation. The court held that the statute that tolled the statute of limitation for foreign corporations that did not have a certificate of authority from the secretary of state violated the Commerce Clause of the U.S. Constitution.

In re Egg Harbor Associates (Bayshore Centre)


A-101


Supreme Court of New Jersey


94 N.J. 358; 464 A.2d 1115; 1983 N.J. LEXIS 2740


May 10, 1983, Argued  
August 1, 1983, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] On certification to the Superior Court, Appellate Division, whose opinion is reported at 185 N.J. Super. 507 (1982).
In re Egg Harbor Associates, 185 N.J. Super. 507, 449 A.2d 1324, 1982 N.J. Super. LEXIS 854 (App.Div., 1982)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant developer challenged the judgment of the Superior Court (New Jersey), which held that the Coastal Area Facility Review Act, N.J. Stat. Ann. §§ 13:19-1 to -21, empowered respondent environmental agency to require low income housing be included in development as a condition for a building permit. Appellant argued that respondent's actions exceeded its authority and were an unconstitutional taking of the property.


OVERVIEW: Appellant developer challenged the judgment of the intermediate appellate court that found that the Coastal Area Facility Review Act (CAFRA), N.J. Stat. Ann. §§ 13:19-1 to -21, empowered respondent environmental agency to require low income housing be included in appellant's development as a condition for a permit. Appellant argued that respondent's actions exceeded its authority and were an unconstitutional taking of the property. Although primarily an environmental act, the court held that the legislative intent for CAFRA, expressly codified, was to promote the public health, safety and welfare and to advance the best long term interest of all people of the state. Holding that mandatory set-asides for lower income housing were constitutional and within the power of respondent and CAFRA, the court found the conditions imposed by respondent were not arbitrary, capricious or unreasonable and that they were supported by substantial credible evidence. Nor was the property "taken" unconstitutionally because appellant did not demonstrate that in complying with the regulations it would not return a profit or a profit so low as to amount to a taking. Judgment for respondent was affirmed.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment of the intermediate appellate court in favor of respondent environmental agency. The court held that the Coastal Area Facility Review Act authorized respondent to require appellant developer to set aside a portion of development for low income housing. The court held that the regulations were lawful, reasonable, and were not an unconstitutional "taking" of the property without compensation.

State, Dept. of Environmental Protection v. Ventron Corp.


A-50/51


Supreme Court of New Jersey


94 N.J. 473; 468 A.2d 150; 1983 N.J. LEXIS 2744; 13 ELR 20837; 19 ERC (BNA) 1505


January 10, 1983, Argued  
July 21, 1983, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1]
Related proceeding at Morton Int'l v. General Accident Ins. Co. of Am., 266 N.J. Super. 300, 629 A.2d 895, 1991 N.J. Super. LEXIS 416 (App.Div., 1991)


PRIOR HISTORY: On certification to the Superior Court, Appellate Division, whose opinion is reported at 182 N.J. Super. 210 (1982).
State, Dep't of Environmental Protection v. Ventron Corp., 94 N.J. 254, 463 A.2d 893, 1983 N.J. LEXIS 3548 (1983)



DISPOSITION:  The court reversed an attorney fees award to one defendant corporation, but otherwise affirmed the judgment. Defendant corporations caused mercury waste to seep into a nearby creek, so they were properly held liable under the Spill Compensation and Control Act. The Act could be applied retroactively because its provisions provided for such application.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant corporations appealed a decision from the Superior Court, Appellate Division (New Jersey), which held them jointly and severally liable for environmental cleanup costs and damages in plaintiff Department of Environmental Protection's action initiated under the Spill Compensation and Control Act, N.J. Stat. Ann. § 58:10-23.11 et seq.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiff Department of Environmental Protection discovered a high level of toxic waste, primarily mercury, in a creek. After investigating the contamination, it was discovered that it was caused by the mercury processing operations conducted on a nearby site for 50 years. Plaintiff initiated an action against defendant corporations for the cost of clean up and removal of such pollution under the Spill Compensation and Control Act, N.J. Stat. Ann. § 58:10-23.11 et seq. The trial court held that defendant corporations were jointly and severally liable and imposed retroactive liability under the Act. On appeal, the court reversed an attorney fees award to one defendant corporation, but otherwise affirmed the judgment. The Act was properly applied retroactively because its provisions specifically allowed such application when a discharge created a substantial and imminent threat to the environment. The mercury discharge created such a threat. Defendant corporations intentionally permitted the mercury to escape into the ground, so they were properly held liable.


OUTCOME: The court reversed an attorney fees award to one defendant corporation, but otherwise affirmed the judgment. Defendant corporations caused mercury waste to seep into a nearby creek, so they were properly held liable under the Spill Compensation and Control Act. The Act could be applied retroactively because its provisions provided for such application.

 

280 of 2470 DOCUMENTS


Town Tobacconist v. Kimmelman


A-65


Supreme Court of New Jersey


94 N.J. 85; 462 A.2d 573; 1983 N.J. LEXIS 2729


January 10, 1983, Argued  
July 19, 1983, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] On certification to the Superior Court, Appellate Division, whose opinion is reported at 186 N.J. Super. 449 (1982).
Town Tobacconist v. Kimmelman, 186 N.J. Super. 449, 453 A.2d 209, 1982 N.J. Super. LEXIS 936 (App.Div., 1982)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellants, a group of retail merchants, sought review of a decision of the Superior Court, Appellate Division (New Jersey), which reversed the trial court's ruling that the Drug Paraphernalia Act (Act), N.J. Stat. Ann. §§ 24:21-1 to -53, was unconstitutionally vague in that the Act's definition of drug paraphernalia was unclear.


OVERVIEW: Appellants, a group of retail merchants whose business fell within the proscriptions of New Jersey's Drug Paraphernalia Act, N.J. Stat. Ann. §§ 24:21-1 to -53, claimed that the Act was void for vagueness, was unconstitutionally overbroad, and chilled their First Amendment, U.S. Const. amend. I, rights. The trial court ruled that the Act was unconstitutionally vague, but the court below reversed. On appeal, the court concluded that the Act was not unconstitutionally vague. It was the defendant's intention and knowledge, and only the defendant's, that would trigger responsibility under the Act. The Act gave not only fair notice, but clear notice. Under the Act, anything capable of adaptation for use with controlled dangerous substances was drug paraphernalia. The issue for law enforcement officers was what the owner or operator's intention or knowledge was, to be determined by all of the evidence, not simply the nature of the goods, but also the manner in which they were displayed, advertised, and all other circumstances that logically bore on the intention or knowledge of the party who was the potential defendant. The court affirmed the decision of the court below.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the decision of the court below, which reversed the trial court's ruling that New Jersey's Drug Paraphernalia Act (Act) was unconstitutionally vague and overbroad. The Act gave fair and clear notice and allowed law enforcement officials to make a determination as to what appellants' intention or knowledge was.

 

281 of 2470 DOCUMENTS


State v. Fusco


A-108


Supreme Court of New Jersey


93 N.J. 578; 461 A.2d 1169; 1983 N.J. LEXIS 2723


May 9, 1983, Argued  
July 14, 1983, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] On certification to the Superior Court, Appellate Division.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant challenged the decision of the Appellate Division of the Superior Court (New Jersey), which affirmed his murder conviction and upheld a sequestration order that prohibited defendant from discussing his trial testimony with counsel during an overnight recess. Defendant argued that the sequestration order violated his state and federal constitutional right to the assistance of counsel.


OVERVIEW: Prior to an overnight recess during defendant's ongoing testimony, the trial judge reminded counsel that a previously entered sequestration order prevented him from discussing testimony with his client. The appellate court affirmed defendant's murder conviction, because the incorrect restriction of defendant's communication with his attorney did not result in prejudice. On review, the court reversed and held that the order that prohibited defendant from discussing his testimony with counsel during the overnight recess violated his right to assistance of counsel under the federal and state constitutions. The primary purpose of sequestration, which prevented a witness from being exposed to other witnesses' testimony, could not apply to a criminal defendant in light of his constitutional right to be present during his entire trial. The court further held that the court-imposed restriction constituted a deprivation of a right so fundamental that it was reversible error and prejudice did not need to be shown. The state failed to meet the heavy burden of proof required to establish that defendant waived his right to counsel. Accordingly, the matter was remanded for a new trial.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the decision that affirmed defendant's murder conviction and upheld a sequestration order that prevented defendant from discussing his trial testimony with counsel during an overnight recess. The court held that the restriction on defendant's communication with counsel constituted a deprivation of a right so fundamental that it was reversible error and prejudice need not be shown. The case was remanded for a new trial.

 

282 of 2470 DOCUMENTS


Salorio v. Glaser


A-33


Supreme Court of New Jersey


93 N.J. 447; 461 A.2d 1100; 1983 N.J. LEXIS 2715


December 6, 1982, Argued  
June 8, 1983, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] On certification to the Superior Court, Chancery Division.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: On remand from the supreme court, the Superior Court, Chancery Division (New Jersey) determined that the Emergency Transportation Tax Act (ETT) did not violate the Privileges and Immunities Clause, U.S. Const. art. IV, § 2, cl. 1, in an action challenging the constitutionality of the ETT filed by plaintiffs, New York commuters against defendant director of taxation. The supreme court certified the matter for direct review on its own motion.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiffs, New York commuters, filed suit against defendant director of taxation for declaratory and injunctive relief. Plaintiffs claimed that the Emergency Transportation Tax Act (ETT), N.J. Stat. Ann. §§ 54:8A-1 to -57, an obligation levied on the New Jersey-derived income of only New York residents, discriminated unconstitutionally against them. On review, the supreme court found that the ETT did not violate the Equal Protection Clause, U.S. Const. amend. XIV, § 1. The matter was remanded to the trial court for plenary hearings on whether the ETT contravened the Privileges and Immunities Clause, U.S. Const. art. IV, § 2, cl. 1. The trial court held that there was no violation and the case was certified for direct review a motion by the supreme court. The supreme court concluded that the ETT violated the privileges and immunities clause. There was no substantial relationship between the tax imposed on plaintiffs and the burden imposed by them on the state's transportation system where the evidence showed that net ETT receipts exceeded plaintiffs' share of transportation costs. Under the facts, reimbursement was not warranted and the ruling was made effective prospectively.


OUTCOME: The supreme court reversed the judgment, holding that the Emergency Transportation Tax Act (ETT) violated the Privileges and Immunities Clause by imposing a tax burden on plaintiffs, New York commuters, which did not bear a substantial relationship to the state's transpiration costs. However, plaintiffs were not entitled to reimbursement and the ruling was made prospectively effective. On remand, judgment was to be entered accordingly.

 

283 of 2470 DOCUMENTS


State v. Williams


A-140/141


Supreme Court of New Jersey


93 N.J. 39; 459 A.2d 641; 1983 N.J. LEXIS 2369; 9 Media L. Rep. 1585


February 23, 1983, Argued  
April 26, 1983, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] On appeal from the Superior Court, Appellate Division (State v. Williams). On certification to the Superior Court, Law Division, Warren County (State v. Koedatich).


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendants were each being tried for separate murders and sought closure proceedings because of adverse pretrial publicity. The Superior Court (New Jersey) in one cause denied one defendant's motion, while the Superior Court, Warren County (New Jersey) denied the other defendant's motion but, thereafter, approved leave to appeal to the state supreme court, which granted an interim order allowing closure.


OVERVIEW: Defendants were each arrested in connection with separate incidents, both involving the stabbing deaths of women. Defendants each filed motions to close proceedings to the public and press asserting that pretrial publicity would deprive them of a fair trial before an impartial jury. The motions were granted. Upon review, the court noted that the issue was whether the public and press had a right of access to pretrial proceedings while still assuring a constitutional requirement of a fair trial before an impartial jury. The court prescribed a balancing test to be used in resolving applications for closure. The court held that the trial court had to determine the likelihood of prejudice and the need for closure based upon relevant evidence that could impinge on a fair trial and selection of an impartial jury. Further, to accommodate the competing concerns of a right of access and the right to a fair trial, the trial court must be satisfied that the need for closure was shown by a preponderance of the evidence. The court vacated the closure orders instituted in defendant's causes and directed the transcripts of the proceedings to be released to the public and the press.


OUTCOME: The court vacated the orders requiring closure of the proceedings in defendants' cases and directed that the transcripts be released to the public and the press because the public and the press had a protectable constitutional interest in access to all pretrial proceedings in the prosecution of the criminal cases.

Perna v. Pirozzi


A-18


Supreme Court of New Jersey


92 N.J. 446; 457 A.2d 431; 1983 N.J. LEXIS 2354; 39 A.L.R.4th 1018


October 25, 1982, Argued  
March 2, 1983, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] On certification to the Superior Court, Appellate Division.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellants patient and wife challenged an order by the Appellate Division of the Superior Court (New Jersey), which denied their right to adequately cross-examine bias witnesses at trial. Appellant claimed that the court improperly restricted their right to adequately cross-examine appellee doctor's witnesses, and that it also denied them equal protection under the law.


OVERVIEW: Appellant patient was scheduled for surgery and he participated in routine pre-admission procedures where he signed the informed consent forms. Subsequently he suffered setbacks after surgery; he was readmitted to the hospital. After readmission appellant learned that his own doctor had not performed his surgery. Appellant claimed that he was subjected to a battery because strange doctor subjected him to unwanted touching, or a violation of the informed consent law or the physician had purposely deceived him. The trial court allowed pre-certification panel's report into evidence, but it would not allow appellants to cross-examine any of the panel on their biases in favor of appellant's doctor whom some of the panel admitted to knowing. The court reversed and remanded for a new trial. It held that the trial court improperly withheld appellant's right to impeach the panel, and that the switch in doctors without appellant's permission amounted to either battery, informed consent violation, or to purposeful deception on part of hospital or doctors or both. The court said that no equal protection claim existed because appellant was not part of a recognized group.


OUTCOME: The court reversed and remanded for a new trial holding that the appellants, patient and wife had not received a fair trial. It concluded that at the very least appellees doctors had violated the medical code of informed consent, and at most had perpetrated a battery on the patient, and appellants could amend their pleadings to conform to the court's opinion.

Callen v. Sherman's, Inc.


A-22


Supreme Court of New Jersey


92 N.J. 114; 455 A.2d 1102; 1983 N.J. LEXIS 2339


October 13, 1982, Argued  
February 10, 1983, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] On certification to the Superior Court, Appellate Division, whose opinion is reported at 182 N.J. Super. 438 (1982).
Callen v. Sherman's, Inc., 182 N.J. Super. 438, 442 A.2d 626, 1982 N.J. Super. LEXIS 672 (N.J. Super. Ct., 1982)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: On certification to the Superior Court, Appellate Division (New Jersey), appellant landlords sought review of the judgment holding that the distraint of appellee tenant's property constituted state action, deprived appellee of due process, and that statute authorizing such action was unconstitutional.


OVERVIEW: Appellant landlord filed a complaint seeking damages from appellee tenants for breach of a commercial lease. Thereafter, appellants engaged a municipal constable to distrain appellees' goods by padlocking his store. Appellees filed a counterclaim asserting that the distraint was unconstitutional. The trial court sustained appellants' motion to dismiss appellees' counterclaim. The appellate court affirmed appellants' damages award, but reversed the dismissal of the counterclaim. The appellate court found that the distress constituted state action and that appellees were not afforded due process. Although the court concluded that the statutes pertaining to distraint were unconstitutional, it reversed the appellate court's judgment finding that appellees did not suffer damages as a result of the distraint. The court determined that the distraint by the constable constituted state action entitling appellees to procedural due process under the U.S. Const. amend. XIV. The distraint statute did not have a procedure for taking property, and by depriving appellees of its goods without notice and hearing, appellants violated appellee's right to due process.


OUTCOME: The court concluded that the statutes pertaining to distraint were unconstitutional because the distraint by the constable constituted state action entitling appellees to procedural due process. Because the distraint statute did not have a procedure for taking property, and it deprived appellees of its goods without notice and hearing, appellants violated appellees' right to due process.

On certification to the Superior Court, Appellate Division, whose opinion is reported at 173 N.J. Super. 45 (1980) (A-37 Round Valley, Inc., etc. v. Township of Clinton,  [***2] etc., et al.).
Urban League of Essex County v. Mahwah, 84 N.J. 390, 420 A.2d 317, 1980 N.J. LEXIS 1976 (1980)
Round Valley, Inc. v. Clinton, 173 N.J. Super. 45, 413 A.2d 356, 1980 N.J. Super. LEXIS 487 (App.Div., 1980)
Urban League of Greater New Brunswick v. Carteret, 170 N.J. Super. 461, 406 A.2d 1322, 1979 N.J. Super. LEXIS 898 (App.Div., 1979)
Glenview Dev. Co. v. Franklin Township, 164 N.J. Super. 563, 397 A.2d 384, 1978 N.J. Super. LEXIS 1217 (Law Div., 1978)
Southern Burlington County NAACP v. Mt. Laurel, 161 N.J. Super. 317, 391 A.2d 935, 1978 N.J. Super. LEXIS 1003 (Law Div., 1978)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiffs, prospective residents and a mobile home developer, sought consolidated review of orders of the trial courts (New Jersey), which upheld defendant city's amended zoning ordinance that purported to provide for the city's "fair share" of lower income housing. The revision had been adopted on remand.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiffs were lower income persons seeking housing in the city. The mobile home developer was permitted to intervene as plaintiff. They attacked the city's amended zoning ordinance passed in response to the previous case, which invalidated those portions of the existing zoning ordinance inconsistent with it. Instead of attempting to amend those deficiencies, the city simply added three new zones to meet its fair share obligation. The trial courts concluded that the city acted in good faith and with the express intent of compliance. The court reversed and remanded, finding that the amended ordinance fell short of what was required; that it neither corrected the deficiencies of the prior ordinance nor otherwise affirmatively provided a realistic opportunity for the city's fair share of lower income housing. The court held that satisfaction of the Mount Laurel obligation was to be determined solely on an objective basis: if the city had in fact provided a realistic opportunity for the construction of its fair share of low and moderate income housing, it had met the Mount Laurel obligation to satisfy the constitutional requirement; if it had not, then it had failed to satisfy it.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed as to the builder's remedy in one case, but reversed the orders upholding the amended zoning ordinance and remanded to the trial courts for further proceedings.

Right to Choose v. Byrne


A-99/100


Supreme Court of New Jersey


91 N.J. 287; 450 A.2d 925; 1982 N.J. LEXIS 2187


May 3, 1982, Argued  
August 18, 1982, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] On certification to the Superior Court, Chancery Division, whose opinions are reported at 165 N.J. Super. 443 (1979), 169 N.J. Super. 543 (1979) and 173 N.J. Super. 66 (1979).
Right to Choose v. Byrne, 173 N.J. Super. 66, 413 A.2d 366, 1980 N.J. Super. LEXIS 477 (N.J. Super. Ct., 1980)
Right to Choose, etc. v. Byrne, 169 N.J. Super. 543, 405 A.2d 427, 1979 N.J. Super. LEXIS 854 (N.J. Super. Ct., 1979)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant state officials and plaintiffs, pregnant women and abortion and welfare rights associations, both sought review of an order by the Superior Court, Chancery Division (New Jersey), which found that N.J. Stat. Ann. § 30:4D-6.1 (1981) violated the due process and equal protection clauses of N.J. Const. art. I, para. 1, and U.S. Const. amend. IV, and awarded plaintiffs' counsel fees under 42 U.S.C.S. § 1488.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiffs, pregnant women and abortion and welfare rights associations, brought suit against defendant state officials and contended that N.J. Stat. Ann. § 30:4D-6.1 (1981), which prohibited Medicaid funding for abortions except where it was indicated to be necessary to preserve the woman's life, was unconstitutional under N.J. Const. art. I, para. 1 and U.S. Const. amend. XIV. The trial court declared that § 30:4D-6.1 invalid and awarded plaintiffs counsel fees under 42 U.S.C.S. § 1488. On appeal, the court modified and affirmed the trial court. The court noted that the United State Supreme Court held that Medicaid funds could be limited to use for abortions only when the life of the mother was in danger. However, the court held that under the state constitution there was no rational basis to make a differentiation between life and health of the mother and held that Medicaid funds for abortion must be provided to protect the health of the mother as well as her life. The court held that Medicaid funds did not have to be provided for non-therapeutic abortions. The court denied attorney's fees to plaintiffs because plaintiffs did not prevail on their federal claims.


OUTCOME: The court modified and affirmed the trial court order in favor of plaintiffs, pregnant women and abortion and welfare rights associations, and against defendant state officials. The court held that under the state constitution the state could not deny Medicaid funds for abortion if the life or health of the mother needed protection, but could deny funds for elective non-therapeutic abortions.

N.J. State Bd. of Higher Educ. v. Bd. of Dirs. of Shelton College


A-138


Supreme Court of New Jersey


90 N.J. 470; 448 A.2d 988; 1982 N.J. LEXIS 2185


March 8, 1982, Argued  
August 9, 1982, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] On certification to the Superior Court, Chancery Division.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant sectarian college challenged the order of the Superior Court, Chancery Division (New Jersey), which affirmed the grant of respondent state board of education's request for an injunction against appellant that prohibited it from offering credits or degrees without licensure by respondent.


OVERVIEW: Respondent state board of education terminated appellant sectarian college's power to confer baccalaureate degrees because appellant failed to comply with statutory minimum requirements. Appellant challenged the constitutionality of the regulatory statutes but on review the court upheld them and affirmed respondent's actions. Appellant later submitted a new application for authorization to award the degrees and began to offer courses that would lead to a bachelor's degree before it received authorization. Respondent sought an injunction that was granted. Appellant challenged the injunction and the appellate division upheld the injunctive relief. A concurrent federal action on these issues was stayed until the state action was completed. The court certified the matter and affirmed the injunction. The court held application of these statutes to a sectarian college whose religious doctrine precluded state licensure did not violate U.S. Const. amend. I. The state's program that licensed institutions of higher education facially did not unduly interfere with the free exercise of religion nor create an excessive state entanglement with religion.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the injunction and held appellant sectarian college could not offer credits or degrees without first having obtained licensure by respondent state board of education because the statutory minimum requirements were constitutional as a minimum intrusion on First Amendment rights, the state had a legitimate interest in education, and the statute was applicable to appellant.

Uricoli v. Board of Trustees


A-53


Supreme Court of New Jersey


91 N.J. 62; 449 A.2d 1267; 1982 N.J. LEXIS 2175


November 17, 1981, Argued  
August 5, 1982, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] On certification to the Superior Court, Appellate Division.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner former police officer sought review of a judgment of the Superior Court, Appellate Division (New Jersey), which affirmed a determination by respondent Board of Trustees, Police and Firemen's Retirement System that denied petitioner's application for accidental disability retirement benefits because of his conviction for one count of malfeasance in office.


OVERVIEW: Petitioner former police officer was given a suspended sentence and dismissed as police chief for a single count of malfeasance in office for fixing a traffic ticket. Respondent Board of Trustees, Police and Firemen's Retirement System denied petitioner's application for accidental disability retirement benefits and held that petitioner did not meet the prerequisite of honorable service for a pension. The lower court affirmed. On review, the court reversed and remanded the case to respondent. The legislature addressed the consequences of misconduct by public employees in various statutes, including N. J. Stat. Ann. § 2C:51-2a(1)-(2). Because the legislature provided specific reference to forfeiture in some pension statutes and not in others, absolute forfeiture was not warranted absent clear legislative intent. The court used a balancing test that incorporated 11 factors and found that the single act of malfeasance after 20 years of honorable service, the absence of a pattern of misconduct, lack of personal gain, and the imposition of adequate criminal sanctions did not support automatic forfeiture of all benefits. Partial forfeiture was to be measured from the date of misconduct.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the judgment, which affirmed the denial of petitioner former police officer's application for accidental disability retirement benefits and the case was remanded to respondent Board of Trustees, Police and Firemen's Retirement System. The court found that absolute forfeiture was not consistent with legislative intent in providing public pensions in the context of contemporary public employment.

In re Hinds


D-16


Supreme Court of New Jersey


90 N.J. 604; 449 A.2d 483; 1982 N.J. LEXIS 2184


February 9, 1982, Argued  
August 4, 1982, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] On certification to District VIII Ethics Committee.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant attorney challenged the filing of disciplinary charges by the District VIII Ethics Committee (New Jersey), which recommended that appellant be charged with violations of N.J. Ct. R., R. Prof. Conduct 1.102(a)(5), and 7.107(d), because appellant made out-of-court statements publicly critical of a trial judge's conduct of an ongoing criminal trial.


OVERVIEW: Appellant attorney held a press conference and harshly criticized the trial judge's conduct of an ongoing criminal trial. Appellant had previously represented the defendant in a civil case and assisted the attorneys in the criminal trial although appellant was not attorney of record at the time of the press conference. An ethics committee recommended that appellant be charged with violations of N.J. Ct. R., R. Prof. Conduct 1.102(a)(5), and 7.107(d). Appellant filed suit to challenge the constitutionality of the disciplinary rules. The court ordered certification of the ethics complaint and directed the entire record be considered by the court. The court held that record was insufficient to make factual determinations in the case and dismissed the charges, but made certain rulings to explain the appropriate ethics principles that would be applied prospectively only. The court concluded that the reasonable likelihood standard of 7.107(D) was constitutional as applied to the extrajudicial statements of an attorney who was associated with a criminal trial, where the statements were about the trial and were intended to be disseminated publicly.


OUTCOME: The court dismissed the ethics charges against appellant attorney because the record was insufficient to make factual determinations in the case. The court made rulings to explain the appropriate ethics principles involved and ordered that the principles would be applied retroactively only.

Birchwood Lakes Colony Club v. Medford Lakes


A-107


Supreme Court of New Jersey


90 N.J. 582; 449 A.2d 472; 1982 N.J. LEXIS 2176; 13 ELR 20115


April 20, 1982, Argued  
August 3, 1982, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] On certification to the Superior Court, Appellate Division, whose opinion is reported at 179 N.J. Super. 409 (1981).
Birchwood Lakes Colony Club, Inc. v. Medford Lakes, 179 N.J. Super. 409, 432 A.2d 525, 1981 N.J. Super. LEXIS 603 (N.J. Super. Ct., 1981)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant municipality sought review of a judgment from the Superior Court, Appellate Division (New Jersey), which held it was erroneous for the trial court to exclude instructions on defendant's liability in nuisance for the discharge of effluent from defendant's sewage plant and which held that the New Jersey Tort Claims Act, N.J Stat. Ann. § 59:1-1 et seq., had not provided defendant with immunity from suit.


OVERVIEW: Defendant municipality's sewage plant polluted a lake. After plaintiff property owners brought an action, the appellate court held that it had been erroneous for the trial court to exclude instructions on defendant's liability in nuisance and also held that defendant was not immune from suit under the New Jersey Tort Claims Act (Act), N.J Stat. Ann. § 59:1-1 et seq. Defendant sought review, and the court affirmed. Although the Act had not expressly authorized nuisance actions against public entities, the court interpreted N.J. Stat. Ann. §§ 59:4-2, 59:2-2 and held that public entity liability for nuisance existed under the Act. The court also found that defendant had not been entitled to plan and design immunity under N.J. Stat. Ann. § 59:4-6, nor was defendant entitled to discretionary immunity under N.J. Stat. Ann. § 59:2-3. The court found defendant was not entitled to plan and design immunity because defendant had yet to prove damages had resulted from a specific plan or design. Similarly, the court found defendant was not entitled to discretionary immunity because defendant had yet to show an exercise of discretion at the planning level, rather than the operational level.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed a judgment holding it was erroneous to exclude instructions on defendant municipality's liability in nuisance because public entity liability for nuisance was recognized under the Tort Claims Act. The court also held defendant was not immune from suit because defendant had not proven design immunity, nor entitlement to discretionary immunity.

 

292 of 2470 DOCUMENTS


In re Polk


A-89


Supreme Court of New Jersey


90 N.J. 550; 449 A.2d 7; 1982 N.J. LEXIS 2169


January 25, 1982, Argued  
July 30, 1982, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] On certification to the Superior Court, Appellate Division, whose opinion is reported at 178 N.J. Super. 191 (1981).


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The State Board of Medical Examiners (Board) sought review from the Superior Court, Appellate Division (New Jersey), which reversed the Board's decision to revoke respondent physician's license to practice medicine. The appellate court concluded that the evidence had not clearly and convincingly established that revocation was the appropriate sanction for respondent's violations of various state statutes.


OVERVIEW: Respondent physician violated numerous statutes regulating the practice of medicine, N.J. Stat. Ann. § 45, et seq. The State Board of Medical Examiners (Board) revoked respondent's license to practice medicine. The appellate court reversed and concluded that the evidence had not clearly and convincingly established that revocation was the appropriate sanction. In addressing the procedural due process rights affected by the administrative license revocation hearing, the court held that a balancing test was necessary to examine the nature of the private interest affected by the proceeding; the countervailing governmental interest to be furthered by the proceeding; and the risk of error in the ultimate determination created by the use of the particular burden of proof. The court held that respondent's interest was substantial and that the state had a substantial interest in regulating the practice of medicine. The court held that the application of the preponderance of the evidence standard to such proceedings did not violate respondent's constitutional rights. The court modified a judgment of the appellate court and remanded to the Board.


OUTCOME: The court modified and remanded judgment of the appellate court, which reversed the Board of Medical Examiners' decision to revoke respondent physician's medical license after he violated statutes. The court held that respondent's interest in his license was substantial but that the state had a substantial interest in regulating medical practice and that preponderance of the evidence was an appropriate standard.

Jordan v. Horsemen's Benevolent & Protective Asso.


A-139


Supreme Court of New Jersey


90 N.J. 422; 448 A.2d 462; 1982 N.J. LEXIS 2168


March 9, 1982, Argued  
July 27, 1982, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] On certification to the Superior Court, Chancery Division.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant horsemen challenged an order of the Superior Court (New Jersey), which granted summary judgment in favor of appellee association concluding that the special law provisions of the New Jersey Constitution had not been violated. Appellants claimed that the law was a special law with an improper classification.


OVERVIEW: Appellant horsemen filed suit seeking a declaratory judgment that the designation of appellee association violated the state constitution. Summary judgment was denied and appellants' complaint was dismissed. Appellants sought review. The court found that appellants were directly affected and had standing. The court found that the object of the statute was to benefit all horsemen, and that the distinction in allocating benefits between members and non-members of appellee bore no rational relationship to any legitimate public purpose. The court held that the state association could not, on the basis of membership, deny New Jersey horsemen the right to participate in the benevolent programs financed by the statutory allotment. The court construed the statute to impose restrictions upon the state association's use and accounting of allocated funds, and found that as a means of funding programs to benefit those employed in thoroughbred racing in New Jersey, the statute was constitutional. The court modified and affirmed the order of the chancery court.


OUTCOME: The trial court's grant of summary judgment dismissing two counts of appellants horsemen's complaint was modified and affirmed because no intended beneficiary was excluded irrationally from the benefits of the statute and no undue or exclusive privilege was granted to appellee association.

Enourato v. New Jersey Bldg. Authority


A-158


Supreme Court of New Jersey


90 N.J. 396; 448 A.2d 449; 1982 N.J. LEXIS 2164


March 22, 1982, Argued  
July 22, 1982, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] On appeal from the Superior Court, Appellate Division, whose opinion is reported at 182 N.J. Super. 58 (1981).


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant taxpayer sought review from an order of the Superior Court, Appellate Division (New Jersey), which affirmed the trial court's dismissal of appellant's suit. Appellant had challenged the constitutionality of provisions of the New Jersey Building Authority Act, N.J. Stat. Ann. 52:18A-78.1 to .32, which gave the legislature the power to veto building projects and lease agreements proposed by respondent building authority.


OVERVIEW: Appellant taxpayer, who leased land to the state, challenged the constitutionality of the New Jersey Building Authority Act (act), N.J. Stat. Ann. 52:18A-78.1 to .32, which gave the legislature the power to veto building projects and lease agreements proposed by respondent building authority. The trial court dismissed the suit and the appellate division affirmed. Appellant sought review and the trial court affirmed the judgment, holding that the act did not violate the separation of powers, N.J. Const. art. III, cl. 1, the Presentment clause, art. V, §1, cl. 14, or the debt limitations clause, N.J. Const. art. VIII, § 2, cl. 3. The court also held that respondent building authority could issue bonds and notes and negotiate lease agreements with state agencies under the act. The court determined that because the act did not authorize the creation of any debts by the state, the debt limitations clause did not apply to respondent building authority's debts or any obligations of the state on its lease agreements. In so finding, the court expressly overruled McCutcheon v. State Building Authority, 13 N.J. 46 (1953).


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment of the appellate division and held that the New Jersey Building Authority Act (act) did not violate the separation of powers, the Presentment Clause, or the debt limitations clause of the New Jersey Constitution. The court also held that respondent building authority could issue bonds and notes and negotiate lease agreements with state agencies under the procedures set forth in the act.

General Assembly of New Jersey v. Byrne


A-176


Supreme Court of New Jersey


90 N.J. 376; 448 A.2d 438; 1982 N.J. LEXIS 2165


May 4, 1982, Argued  
July 22, 1982, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] On certification to the Superior Court, Appellate Division.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant, the New Jersey General Assembly, brought declaratory judgment actions against respondent, the New Jersey governor and certain administrators, seeking a determination that the New Jersey Legislative Oversight Act, N.J. Stat. Ann. §§ 52:14B-4.1 to -4.9 (1981) was constitutional, and the court granted respondents' motion for direct certification of the consolidated case from the Superior Court, Appellate Division (New Jersey).


OVERVIEW: The court struck down the New Jersey Legislative Oversight Act (act), N.J. Stat. Ann. §§ 52:14B-4.1 to -4.9 (1981) as unconstitutional because it violated the separation of powers under N.J. Const. art. III, para. 1 (1947). The court found that the act allowed appellant legislature to veto by a concurrent resolution any rule proposed by a state agency after the act's passage, with certain exceptions. The court found that the act's grant of power to appellant to revoke at will portions of coherent regulatory schemes violated the separation of powers by excessively interfering with the constitutional mandate of respondent governor to execute the law faithfully. The court held that the act's legislative veto provision further offended the separation of powers by effectively allowing appellant to amend or repeal existing laws without respondent governor's participation. This process also contravened the requirement in N.J. Const. art. V, § 1, para. 14 (1947) that changes in legislative policy be effected by a majority vote of appellant and approval by respondent governor or, after executive veto, by a two-thirds vote of appellant.


OUTCOME: The court ruled that the New Jersey Legislative Oversight Act was unconstitutional because it violated the separation of powers doctrine by giving appellant legislature excessive power to impede respondent governor's ability to execute the law and allowed appellant to effectively amend or repeal existing laws without respondent's participation and such impingement was unconstitutional the New Jersey constitution.

In re Application of Boardwalk Regency Corp. for Casino License


A-127/128/129/161


Supreme Court of New Jersey


90 N.J. 361; 447 A.2d 1335; 1982 N.J. LEXIS 2162


March 22, 1982, Argued  
July 21, 1982, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Appeal dismissed by Perlman v. Attorney Gen. of New Jersey, 459 U.S. 1081, 103 S. Ct. 562, 74 L. Ed. 2d 927, 1982 U.S. LEXIS 4671 (1982)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] On appeal from and certification to the Superior Court, Appellate Division, whose opinion is reported at 180 N.J. Super. 324 (1981).
In re Application of Boardwalk Regency Corp. for Casino License, 180 N.J. Super. 324, 434 A.2d 1111, 1981 N.J. Super. LEXIS 658 (N.J. Super. Ct., 1981)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant directors challenged a judgment from the Superior Court (New Jersey), which ruled that if they were not removed from positions of control in the extensive corporate hierarchy of a holding company and its subsidiaries, then a gaming application would be denied, pursuant to the Casino Control Act, N.J. Stat. Ann. § 5:12-1 to -152. Appellants did not satisfy the standards as casino key employees.


OVERVIEW: In reassessing appellant directors' protest of the requirements under which they would be granted a gaming license, the court found that the final adjudication of the issues was a matter of considerable importance to the casino industry as well as the general public, because appellants had considerable influence. The court found that appellants failed to make a demonstration of good character in fact. Appellants maintained that the Commission's order unconstitutionally conditioned their licensure on the divestiture of their interests in non-New Jersey subsidiaries, a challenge that they mounted on the basis of the Commerce Clause, U.S.Const. art. I, § 8, and the Due Process Clause, U.S.Const. amend. XIV. However, the court rejected their arguments and held that the Commission's divestiture order bore a rational relationship to a legitimate state interest, and no fundamental right was affected. The court held that permitting them to remain in or assume a structured, formal relationship of ownership, employment, or management in any subsidiaries, even a non-gaming one, would encourage their exertion of enormous power and influence. The judgment was affirmed as modified.


OUTCOME: After reinstating the condition that appellant directors must not receive any remuneration from subsidiaries outside of New Jersey, the court affirmed the judgment of the appellate court with that modification. All provisions of the Commission's order were reinstated, and the Commission was directed to establish a new timetable for submission of the holding company's plans. A stay was vacated, effective ten days after release of the opinion.

State v. Crawley


A-86


Supreme Court of New Jersey


90 N.J. 241; 447 A.2d 565; 1982 N.J. LEXIS 2157


January 26, 1982, Argued  
July 14, 1982, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] On certification to the Superior Court, Law Division.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff city challenged a ruling from the Superior Court (New Jersey), which dismissed loitering complaints against defendants on the ground that the municipal ordinance had been preempted and rendered unenforceable by the New Jersey Code of Criminal Justice, N.J. Stat. Ann. §§ 2C:1-1 to 98-4 (1979).


OVERVIEW: At issue was whether plaintiff city's municipal loitering ordinance had been preempted by the New Jersey Code of Criminal Justice, N.J. Stat. Ann. §§ 2C:1-1 to 98-4, prohibiting enforcement of the ordinance against defendants. The court's holding rested primarily on the preemption by exclusion clause of N.J. Stat. Ann. § 2C:1-5(d), which prohibited enactment or enforcement of any local ordinance that conflicted with any policy of the state. Given the legislature's comprehensive intervention in the area, the court found the conspicuous absence of a loitering statute significant, because New Jersey's pre-Code criminal law included specific prohibitions on vagrancy and loitering, but those provisions were repealed and not reincorporated into the new code. The court concluded that the absence of a loitering proscription reflected a state policy to decriminalize such activity, so the court held that the preemption clause prohibited enforcement of plaintiff's loitering ordinance and the court affirmed the judgment.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment of the lower court, which ruled that plaintiff city's enforcement of its municipal loitering ordinance against defendants had been preempted by the new state penal code, because there was a conspicuous absence of a loitering statute in the code, considering the legislature's comprehensive intervention in the area.

In re In re Martin


A-84


Supreme Court of New Jersey


90 N.J. 295; 447 A.2d 1290; 1982 N.J. LEXIS 2150


January 11, 1982, Argued  
June 30, 1982, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] On certification to the Casino Control Commission.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellants, applicants for licenses to become casino employees, sought review from the Casino Control Commission (New Jersey), which prohibited appellants from becoming licensed casino employees for failure to submit the required application form, pursuant to the Casino Control Act, N.J. Stat. Ann. § 5:12-1 to -152. Appellants contended that their constitutional and statutory rights were invaded by the comprehensive licensing scheme.


OVERVIEW: Appellants, applicants for licenses to become casino employees, refused to answer certain questions on the applications and contended that their constitutional and statutory rights were invaded by the comprehensive licensing scheme. Pursuant to the Casino Control Act (Act), N.J. Stat. Ann. § 5:12-1 to -152, the Casino Control Commission denied appellants such casino licenses, and appellants sought review. The court held that U.S. Const. amend. IV was not violated by the Act's allowance of warrantless administrative searches. The court held that in comparison to the strong public interest in regulating the gaming industry, appellants' privacy interests and freedom of association rights were not violated by requiring disclosure of certain private information. The court upheld the constitutionality of the statute and regulations but noted that the power of the state to condition employment on disclosure of personal information was not without constitutional limit and established guidelines for the commission to use in the future. The court affirmed and modified a decision of the commission.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed and modified a decision of the Casino Control Commission, which denied appellants, applicants for licenses to become casino employees, such casino licenses for refusing to answer certain application questions, pursuant to the Casino Control Act. The court held that appellants' Fourth Amendment rights were not violated by the allowance of warrantless administrative searches but that the state's power was not without limits.

Harry's Village, Inc. v. Egg Harbor Township


A-41


Supreme Court of New Jersey


89 N.J. 576; 446 A.2d 862; 1982 N.J. LEXIS 2137


November 17, 1981, Argued  
June 7, 1982, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] On certification to the Superior Court, Appellate Division.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant landlord challenged the judgment of the Superior Court, Appellate Division (New Jersey), ruling that a notice to quit was necessary before increasing rent, and reversing that portion of the trial court's ruling that a notice to quit was not required when a rent control ordinance provides tenants adequate notice of board proceedings and rent increases.


OVERVIEW: Appellant landlord obtained a rent increase from a rent control board for capital improvements, notified the tenants of the decision, and filed an action challenging the rent increase as inadequate. The trial court set a new rent increase, and ruled that a notice to quit was not required to make it effective. The court affirmed the rent increase, but held that, after receiving authorization to increase rent, a landlord was required to give a valid notice to quit for the rent increase to be effective in any periodic tenancy, including a tenancy in a rent-controlled municipality, because a municipal rent control ordinance properly required that tenants be notified of a request for a rent increase, but did not protect all the interests covered by the common law notice to quit. Where a landlord had substantially complied with the requirements of a notice to quit and inadvertently failed to give notice on the precise anniversary date, a notice to quit was effective on the next anniversary date after receipt of the notice.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment of the lower court that a notice to quit was necessary before increasing rent, but modified that judgment to allow the notice that was served on the tenants to be effective on the next anniversary date after its receipt, and held that tenants who remained in possession after receipt of the notice to quit and notice of rent increase were liable for increased rents.

Rivera v. Gerner


A-36


Supreme Court of New Jersey


89 N.J. 526; 446 A.2d 508; 1982 N.J. LEXIS 2133


January 11, 1982, Argued  
May 26, 1982, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] On certification to the Superior Court, Appellate Division.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant driver appealed an order of the Superior Court of New Jersey, Appellate Division, which affirmed the trial court's judgment apportioning liability among defendant driver, defendant school bus driver, and defendant board of education, in plaintiff pedestrian's personal injury action. Defendant driver claimed the trial court erred in raising the limitation on public entity liability under the Tort Claims Act, N.J. Stat. Ann. § 59:9-2(d).


OVERVIEW: Defendant driver contested the appellate court's order affirming the trial court's judgment apportioning negligence among defendant driver, defendant school bus driver, and defendant board of education, and limiting defendant school bus driver and board of education's liability in plaintiff pedestrian's personal injury action. Defendant driver claimed that the trial court erred in raising defendant school bus driver and board of education's Tort Claims Act (Act), N.J. Stat. Ann. § 59:9-2(d) defense limiting pain and suffering damages for the first time after summation, and by finding that the Act could not be waived. The appellate court affirmed, but the court reversed the appellate court's order and remanded to the trial court, holding that defendant school bus driver and board of education did not waive limitation of liability under § 59:9-2(d) merely because they did not plead the specific statutory section, and the trial court properly raised this issue on its own. However, asserting the defense after summation in the trial caused surprise and prejudiced defendant driver's opportunity to fully present his case to the jury, and thus the matter was remanded for a new trial.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the appellate court's order affirming the trial court's judgment, and remanded to the trial court, in plaintiff pedestrian's personal injury action against defendant driver, defendant school bus driver, and defendant board of education, and held that the trial court could raise defendant school bus driver and board of education's Tort Claims Act defense, but doing so after summation prejudiced defendant driver's defense.

Smith v. Ricci


A-98


Supreme Court of New Jersey


89 N.J. 514; 446 A.2d 501; 1982 N.J. LEXIS 2135


February 8, 1982, Argued  
May 25, 1982, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] On certification to the State Board of Education.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellants challenged adoption by respondent State Board of Education (New Jersey), of N.J. Admin. Code tit. 6, § 29-7.1 that required local school districts to implement sex education programs, as impinging upon the free exercise of religion, constituting an establishment of religion in violation of U.S. Const. amend. I, and alleged that its adoption did not comply with the Administrative Procedure Act, N.J. Stat. Ann. §§ 52:14B-1 et. seq.


OVERVIEW: Appellants contended that the sex education program ordered by respondent State Board of Education violated the Free Exercise and Establishment Clauses of U.S. Const. amend. I, and that the process of adopting the regulation did not comply with the Administrative Procedure Act, N.J. Stat. Ann. § 52:14B-1 et. seq. The court held that the regulation, because of the excusal clause, did not inhibit the free exercise of religion, and to permit the appellants to control what others may study because the subject may be offensive to their religious or moral scruples would violate the Establishment Clause. The regulation did not require any point of view, secular or religious, to be stressed to the exclusion of others. The court therefore held that the program did not constitute an establishment of religion. Appellants' bare assertion there was no proof the program would be effective did not satisfy appellants' burden of proving that the regulation was unreasonable. The court concluded that the procedures were in accord with the Administrative Procedure Act, and found no procedural irregularity.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the action of respondent State Board of Education that required local school districts to implement sex education programs because, having an excusal clause, the programs did not inhibit the free exercise of religion and, not requiring any point of view to be stressed to the exclusion of others, were not an establishment of religion, and the regulation's adoption did comply with the Administrative Procedure Act.

Application of Forsythe


A-202


Supreme Court of New Jersey


91 N.J. 141; 450 A.2d 499; 1982 N.J. LEXIS 2188


May 11, 1982, Argued  
May 21, 1982, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] On appeal from the Superior Court, Appellate Division, whose opinions are reported at    N.J. Super.     (1982).


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioners, current members of the House of Representatives in Congress, sought review of a decision from the Superior Court, Appellate Division (New Jersey) that sustained the validity of a congressional redistricting statute.


OVERVIEW: Petitioners, current members of the House of Representatives in Congress, challenged a congressional redistricting statute under N.J. Stat. Ann. § 1:7-4 and urged that its passage was not in conformity with the requisites for passing legislation under N.J. Const. art. IV, § 4. The intermediate appellate court sustained the statute's validity, and petitioners sought review. The court affirmed the intermediate appellate court's decision because it held that the statute's passage did not violate N.J. Const. art. IV, § 4. The assembly, by voice vote, moved to substitute bill S-711 for bill A-605 because their identical content. Bill A-605 was not separately read before the assembly and bill S-711 was read but without the requisite one full calendar day between readings. The court held that the mandate of art. IV, § 4 regarding properly timed readings of bills was met where the content of the bills remained unchanged and were before each house for the requisite period of time individually. Given that the purpose of the provision was to ensure legislators the opportunity to become familiar with the contents of a law as it was ultimately enacted, the mandate was met.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the intermediate appellate court's decision to sustain the act passed by the General Assembly and the Senate as constitutionally valid because the legislators were given the opportunity to become familiar with the contents of the law as it was ultimately enacted, meeting the statutory mandate for procedure for passing legislation into law.

Chamber of Commerce v. State


A-24


Supreme Court of New Jersey


89 N.J. 131; 445 A.2d 353; 1982 N.J. LEXIS 1901; 110 L.R.R.M. 2328; 94 Lab. Cas. (CCH) P55,339


December 14, 1981, Argued  
May 3, 1982, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] On certification to the Superior Court, Law Division.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant state appealed the decision of a Superior Court (New Jersey), which granted summary judgment in favor of plaintiffs, federal and state chambers of commerce, in plaintiffs' action seeking declaratory judgment that the Strikebreakers Act, N. J. Stat. Ann. 34:13C-1 et seq., was unconstitutional. The trial court declared N.J. Stat. Ann. §§ 34:13C-1(c), 34:13C-2, 34:13C-3 unconstitutional. Plaintiffs filed a motion for direct certification.


OVERVIEW: The Strikebreakers Act (Act), N.J. Stat. Ann. § 34:13C-1 et seq., was challenged by plaintiffs, federal and state chambers of commerce, in a declaratory action. Plaintiffs filed a motion for direct certification after defendant state appealed the trial court judgment that declared N.J. Stat. Ann. §§ 34:13C-1(c), 34:13C-2, 34:13C-3 unconstitutional. The court affirmed in part and modified the trial court's judgment, holding that the Act was preempted by federal labor law, pursuant to U.S. Const. art. VI, cl. 2, as to employers and employees falling with the National Labor Relations Act (NLRA), 29 U.S.C.§ 151 et seq. Because employers had the right under the federal law to hire replacement employees in the event of a strike, the Act's provisions contravened the labor-management economic balance established by the NLRA. As to noncovered NLRA situations, the court concluded that §§ 34:13C-2, 34:13C-3 were valid, and § 34:13C-1(c) with the excision of the phrase, or to supply from without the state, was likewise valid and did not violate U.S. Const. art. 1, § 8, cl. 3. The court held that the Act did not violate the equal protection concepts of the state and federal constitutions.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed in part and modified the trial court's judgment, that granted summary judgement in favor of plaintiffs, federal and state chambers of commerce, in their declaratory judgment action. The court held that the Strikebreakers Act was preempted by federal labor law, as to employers and employees falling with the National Labor Relations Act because employers had the right under to hire replacement employees in the event of a strike.

In re Garay


A-115/146


Supreme Court of New Jersey


89 N.J. 104; 444 A.2d 1107; 1982 N.J. LEXIS 1894; 32 A.L.R.4th 658


January 25, 1982, Argued  
April 29, 1982, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] On certification to the Superior Court, Appellate Division.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant state challenged the decision of the Superior Court of New Jersey, Appellate Division, which overturned a $ 116,000 penalty that was assessed to respondent doctor in a Medicaid fraud action. Respondent asserted claims of violation of ex post facto law, double jeopardy, and cruel and unusual punishment.


OVERVIEW: Respondent doctor was prosecuted for Medicaid fraud. Appellant state then proceeded civilly against him for reimbursement of the overpayment of Medicaid charges and for damages. The lower court reversed the penalty of $ 2,000 per claim as having been fundamentally unfair, but affirmed other penalties assessed by the director of the State Division of Medical Assistance and Health Services (agency). The state appealed the lower court's decision. The court reversed the lower court and remanded the case to the agency. The court found that the penalties imposed by the agency did not violate the constitutional prohibition against ex post facto laws, double jeopardy, and cruel and unusual punishment because the remedies were civil, not criminal. The court found that the director of the agency had not used his discretion when he assessed respondent the maximum penalty. The court remanded the case so that the director could determine a reasonable sum. The court said that N.J. Stat. Ann. §§ 30:4D-17(e), 30:4D-7(h) provided for recovery of overpayments, treble damages, and payment of interest. Respondent's bankruptcy filing did not preclude collection of the penalties.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the judgment of the lower court which had declared a penalty of $ 2,000 per Medicaid fraud violation against respondent doctor to have been fundamentally unfair. The court remanded the case to the director of the state medical assistance agency so that the director could determine a reasonable amount for the penalties. The court said that civil penalties did not violate respondent's constitutional rights.

On Petition for Review of Opinion 475 of Advisory Committee on Professional Ethics


A-82


Supreme Court of New Jersey


89 N.J. 74; 444 A.2d 1092; 1982 N.J. LEXIS 1898; 33 A.L.R.4th 381


October 5, 1981, Argued  
April 28, 1982, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] On petition for review from the Advisory Committee on Professional Ethics.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner law partnership sought review of an opinion of the Advisory Committee on Professional Ethics, which prohibited it from opening a New Jersey office under a name which included partners who had never been a member of the New Jersey bar, and challenged the constitutionality of N.J. Ct. R. 2-102(C), urging the court to revise the rule or to grant petitioner an exemption from the rule's coverage.


OVERVIEW: Petitioner law firm was prohibited by the Advisory Committee on Professional Ethics from opening a New Jersey office under a name where none of petitioner's named partners had ever been members of the state bar. The court affirmed the opinion, but referred the rule, along with the ban on television advertising, to a special supreme court committee for a report and recommendations. The court held that the danger that deception would occur in the absence of N.J. Ct. R. 2-102(C) was sufficient justification to restrict petitioner's commercial speech. The rule did not violate the Commerce Clause, U.S. Const. art. I, § 8, cl. 3, because it presented little burden on interstate commerce, and any burden was wholly incidental to a valid regulation of the state bar. There was no violation of the Privileges and Immunities Clause, U.S. Const. art. IV, § 2, cl. 1, because the right to use a specific law firm name was not "fundamental." There were significant, rational distinctions between prohibiting the use of a firm name where neither named partner had ever been licensed to practice in New Jersey and requiring that law firms change their names whenever a named partner dies.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the opinion, which prohibited petitioner law partnership from opening a New Jersey office under a name which included partners who had never been a member of the state bar, and denied relief to petitioner, because the Advisory Committee correctly applied the rule, which was constitutional. The court referred the rule, along with the ban on television advertising, to the supreme court committee for report and recommendations.

State v. Humphreys


A-20


Supreme Court of New Jersey


89 N.J. 4; 444 A.2d 569; 1982 N.J. LEXIS 1892


October 6, 1981, Argued  
April 15, 1982, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] On appeal from the Superior Court, Appellate Division.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The state appealed the order of the Superior Court, Appellate Division (New Jersey), that reversed the denial of defendant's motion for conditional discharge, under N.J. Stat. Ann. § 24:21-27, pursuant to his conviction, on a plea of guilty to a charge of possession of marijuana, in violation of N.J. Stat. Ann. § 24:21-21a(4).


OVERVIEW: Defendant pled guilty to possession of marijuana, in violation of N.J. Stat. Ann. § 24:21-21a(4), and applied for a conditional discharge pursuant to N.J. Stat. Ann. § 24:21-27. The trial court denied the motion for a conditional discharge and, instead, sentenced defendant to jail time, probation, and a fine. Defendant appealed and the appellate division affirmed the trial court judgment, but the court granted certification and reversed the appellate division's judgment and remanded to the trial court. The trial court conducted a new hearing and again denied the motion for conditional discharge, which the appellate division then reversed. The state appealed to the court. The court again reversed the appellate division, because it concluded that the trial court did not clearly abuse its discretion in denying defendant a conditional discharge. The court ruled that the whole person, not just his occupation, should be considered in sentencing, and that public sentiment or outrage should not influence a sentencing court's decision. In this case, the evidence supported the judge's decision that defendant was not amenable to rehabilitation or correction of behavior.


OUTCOME: The order that reversed the denial of defendant's motion for conditional discharge was reversed, because there was no clear abuse in the trial judge's denial of the conditional discharge because defendant's actions and statements supported a finding that he was not amenable to rehabilitation or correction in the sense the conditional discharge program contemplated.

 

307 of 2470 DOCUMENTS


United Bldg. & Constr. Trades Council v. Camden


A-79


Supreme Court of New Jersey


88 N.J. 317; 443 A.2d 148; 1982 N.J. LEXIS 1876


December 15, 1981, Argued  
February 18, 1982, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] On certification to the Department of Treasury.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant contractors' association sought review of the decision of respondent New Jersey Department of Treasury, which approved the affirmative action plan enacted by respondent City of Camden pursuant to respondent's affirmative action rules, N.J. Admin. Code §§ 17:27-1.1 to -13.2, which plan established minority hiring goals for city contracts and established a city residency requirement for the labor force on city public works projects.


OVERVIEW: Respondent City of Camden (city) passed an ordinance mandating affirmative action by private construction companies that were awarded public contract works, establishing a 25 percent minority hiring goal for city contracts, and requiring that 40 percent of the labor force in city public works projects be city residents. Both requirements exceeded the minimum requirements set forth in the affirmative action rules of respondent Department of Treasury (department), N.J. Admin. Code tit. 17, §§ 27-1.1 to -13.2. Appellant contractors' association challenged respondent department's approval of the ordinance. The court affirmed, holding that N.J. Stat. Ann. § 10:5-36(c), which required such approval, would be rendered superfluous if local governments had no authority to differ from respondent department's guidelines. The court held that the ordinance's requirements did not impede the promotion of equal opportunity, and in fact, questioned the fairness of any hiring practices that resulted in a work force with less than 25 percent minority workers, given the large minority population of respondent city. The court held that the resident hiring quota was constitutionally valid.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the approval by respondent New Jersey Department of Treasury (department) of the ordinance of respondent City of Camden, that set a minority hiring goal and a residency quota for public work contracts with private contractors, because the ordinance did not impede the promotion of equal opportunity, it could exceed respondent department's affirmative action requirements, and the residency requirement was constitutional.

 

308 of 2470 DOCUMENTS


State v. Cavallo


A-42


Supreme Court of New Jersey


88 N.J. 508; 443 A.2d 1020; 1982 N.J. LEXIS 1886; 42 A.L.R.4th 919


November 17, 1981, Argued  
February 17, 1982, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] On certification to the Superior Court, Appellate Division.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendants appealed the order of the Superior Court, Appellate Division (New Jersey), which affirmed the trial court's refusal to allow expert testimony as to the psychological traits of a rapist and affirmed defendants' convictions for abduction in violation of N.J. Stat. Ann. § 2A:86-1, private lewdness in violation of N.J. Stat. Ann. § 2A:115-1, and rape in violation of N.J. Stat. Ann. § 2A:138-1.


OVERVIEW: Defendants were convicted of abduction under N.J. Stat. Ann. § 2A:86-1, private lewdness under N.J. Stat. Ann. § 2A:115-1, and rape under N.J. Stat. Ann. § 2A:138-1, after the trial court refused to allow defendants' expert witness to testify as to one defendant's lack of the psychological traits of a rapist. The appellate court affirmed the trial court's ruling and the convictions. On appeal, the court affirmed, concluding that defendants did not meet their burden of proof under N.J. R. Evid. 56. The court held that defendants failed to show that the expert's testimony was based on reasonably reliable scientific premises or that the scientific community generally accepted the existence of identifiable character traits common to rapists. Defendants offered no additional expert testimony, did not cite any scientific or legal writings, and failed to show that the proffered evidence had been accepted as reliable by other jurisdictions. The court further ruled that the application of rule 56 did not violate any constitutional right of defendants because the exclusion was based on a determination that the evidence was unreliable.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the appellate court's judgments, finding that defendants did not meet their evidentiary burden of proving that the psychiatrist's testimony regarding the psychological traits of rapists was scientifically reliable. The court also found that the application of the scientific reliability test did not violate defendants' constitutional rights as the testimony was excluded because it was unreliable.

 

309 of 2470 DOCUMENTS


Worthington v. Fauver


A-91


Supreme Court of New Jersey


88 N.J. 183; 440 A.2d 1128; 1982 N.J. LEXIS 1870


October 19, 1981, Argued  
January 6, 1982, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] On appeal from the Superior Court, Appellate Division, whose opinion is reported at 180 N.J. Super. 368 (1981).


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiffs, county executive and sheriff, sought review of the judgment of the Superior Court, Appellate Division (New Jersey), in favor of defendants, the state governor and the Commissioner of Corrections, in plaintiffs' challenge against defendant governor's implementation of the Civil Defense and Disaster Control Act, N.J. Stat. Ann. App.A:9-30 et seq., authorizing defendant commissioner to house state prisoners in county facilities.


OVERVIEW: Due to overcrowding of inmates in state correctional facilities, defendant state governor invoked his power under the Civil Defense and Disaster Control Act (Act), N.J. Stat. Ann. App.A:9-30 et seq., and issued temporary executive orders authorizing defendant Commissioner of Corrections to house state prisoners in county correctional facilities. Plaintiffs, county executive and sheriff, challenged the validity of those orders. The lower court approved the orders, and the court affirmed on appeal. Prison overcrowding was an emergency and defendant governor did not have to wait until the danger actually occurred, as a substantial likelihood of immediate danger was sufficient. The orders were specifically tailored to the magnitude of the crisis and were not arbitrary or capricious. A three-part test was applied to determine that the N.J. Const. art. III, § 1, doctrine of separation of powers was not violated. The legislature delegated authority to act to defendant governor, retained the power to amend the Act, and provided standards to guide the discretionary exercise of the delegated power.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment approving two temporary emergency executive orders issued by defendant state governor that authorized defendant Commissioner of Corrections to house state prisoners in county correctional facilities due to prison overcrowding, as they were authorized under the Civil Defense and Disaster Control Act and were not unconstitutional.

Gilbert v. Gladden


A-112


Supreme Court of New Jersey


87 N.J. 275; 432 A.2d 1351; 1981 N.J. LEXIS 1659


February 10, 1981, Argued  
July 29, 1981, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] On certification to the Superior Court, Law Division.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiffs, senators, general assembly members, and taxpayers, sought review of the decision of the Superior Court (New Jersey), which granted defendants, senate president and general assembly speaker, summary judgment, in plaintiffs' challenge regarding the provision which governed presentment of bills to the governor under N.J. Const. art. V, § 1, para. 14.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiffs, senators, general assembly members, and taxpayers, argued that the practice of gubernatorial courtesy under N.J. Const. art. V, § 1, para. 14 (1947) was a frustration of the legislative process through the "pocket veto" technique involved. They further argued that since the constitution did not permit discretion with regard to the timing of the presentment of bills, presentment could not be forestalled by either the governor or the legislature. Defendants, senate president and general assembly speaker, contended that this presented a nonjusticiable political question. After defendants were granted summary judgment, plaintiffs appealed. The court found that the issue was a nonjusticiable political question. The court held that in absence of a constitutional or statutory standard, it was not the court's function to substitute its judgment for that of the legislature. The court held the timing of presentment of bills was discretionary and such a practice was within the legislature's prerogative. The court held that N.J. Stat. Ann. § 1:2-5 did not abolish gubernatorial courtesy. The court affirmed the trial court's judgment.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the trial court's grant of summary judgment to defendants, senate president and general assembly speaker. The court found that the issue raised by plaintiffs, senators, general assembly members, and taxpayers, with regard to gubernatorial courtesy practice was a nonjusticiable political question. As such, it was not the court's function to substitute its judgment for that of the legislature.
