State v. Ingenito


A-94


Supreme Court of New Jersey


87 N.J. 204; 432 A.2d 912; 1981 N.J. LEXIS 1657


March 10, 1981, Argued  
July 27, 1981, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] On certification to the Superior Court, Appellate Division, whose opinion is reported at 169 N.J. Super. 524 (1979).


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed from a decision of the Superior Court, Appellate Division (New Jersey), which affirmed his conviction for possession of a firearm by a previously convicted felon. Defendant claimed that the state's use of a prior conviction to prove possession amounted to collateral estoppel in violation of his right to a jury trial.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was charged with unlicensed transfer of a weapon and possession of a firearm by a previously convicted felon, and on his motion the counts were severed. He was convicted of the first offense and, over his objection, the state introduced the conviction at his jury trial on the second offense to prove possession. Defendant appealed claiming that such use amounted to collateral estoppel in violation of his constitutional right to trial by jury. The appellate division affirmed and the court granted defendant's petition for certification. It held that the use of defendant's prior conviction in the trial of the second case constituted collateral estoppel against defendant and impinged upon his constitutional right of trial by jury under U.S. Const. amend. VI and N.J. Const. art. I, para. 9 because it was a preclusive device serving to establish affirmatively an essential element of the crime charged which had the effect of creating a near-mandatory presumption in the minds of the jurors as to the guilt of the defendant. The judgment was reversed and the case remanded for a new trial.


OUTCOME: The court held that the state's use of defendant's prior conviction for unlicensed transfer of weapons in the prosecution of the possession charge constituted collateral estoppel against him and impinged on his constitutional right to trial by jury. The conviction was reversed and the case was remanded for a new trial.

State v. Young


A-139


Supreme Court of New Jersey


87 N.J. 132; 432 A.2d 874; 1981 N.J. LEXIS 1663


March 10, 1981, Argued  
July 21, 1981, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] On appeal from the Superior Court, Appellate Division.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The state appealed the decision of the Superior Court, Appellate Division (New Jersey), that reversed the trial court's judgment and vacated defendant's conviction and guilty plea to possession of cocaine with intent to distribute. The appellate court held that the search of defendant was unconstitutional.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was convicted of possession of cocaine with intent to distribute. The court reversed the appellate court's decision that reversed the trial court's judgment that denied defendant's motion to suppress because the troopers had probable cause to search the suitcase found in the defendant's car and therefore, the scope of the search was not unreasonable. The court held that the search came within the automobile exception to the warrant requirement as understood at the time of the search. Accordingly, the evidence obtained in that search need not have been suppressed. The court held that the troopers had information aside from defendant's mere possession of a small amount of marijuana and that information was sufficient to suggest that other crimes were being committed and to constitute probable cause to search the car and its contents. The court held that the troopers did not unreasonably extend the scope of the search to areas of the car and its contents beyond those in which their observations provided sufficient probable cause to suspect evidence of criminal activity.


OUTCOME: The court held that the judgment of the appellate court was reversed and defendant's conviction was reinstated because the troopers had probable cause to search the suitcase found in the defendant's car and therefore, the scope of the search was not unreasonable. The court held that the search came within the automobile exception to the warrant requirement as understood at the time of the search.

Paterson Police PBA Local v. Paterson


A-97


Supreme Court of New Jersey


87 N.J. 78; 432 A.2d 847; 1981 N.J. LEXIS 1671; 112 L.R.R.M. 2205


April 7, 1981, Argued  
July 16, 1981, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] On certification to the Superior Court, Appellate Division.
Paterson Police PBA Local No. 1 v. City of Paterson, 1980 N.J. Super. Unpub. LEXIS 7 (App.Div., June 10, 1980)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant city sought review of the decision of the Superior Court, Appellate Division (New Jersey), that ruled in favor of respondent police union in respondent's action against appellant for breach of the collective negotiation agreement with appellant regarding the filling of vacancies. The lower court held that the promotion clause in the dispute came within the permissive category and was therefore enforceable if included in the agreement.


OVERVIEW: Respondent police union filed a grievance against appellant city claiming a breach of the collective negotiation agreement by appellant. The court reversed the lower court's decision that the promotion clause came within the permissive category and was therefore enforceable because the promotion clause of the agreement was not permissively negotiable and was unenforceable as outside the scope of negotiations. The court held that the promotion clause deprived appellant of the discretion to diminish the police force or to deploy officers as it deemed best. The agreement precluded appellant from leaving positions in the police department vacant. The court held that although N.J. Stat. Ann. 40A:14-143 did not refer directly to filling of vacancies by promotion, it revealed a clear legislative intent to vest discretion in a municipality to demote or lay-off police officers for economic reasons. The court held that a municipality should also retain the discretion to forgo for economic reasons the filling of vacancies by promotions. The promotion clause requirement that all vacancies be filled within 60 days placed substantial limitations on appellant's policy-making authority.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the lower court's decision that the promotion clause in the dispute came within the permissive category and was enforceable because the promotion clause of the agreement, was not permissively negotiable and was unenforceable as outside the scope of negotiations.

Westfield Ctr. Serv. v. Cities Serv. Oil Co.


A-57


Supreme Court of New Jersey


86 N.J. 453; 432 A.2d 48; 1981 N.J. LEXIS 1646


February 9, 1981, Argued  
June 29, 1981, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] On certification to the Superior Court Appellate Division, whose opinion is reported at 172 N.J. Super. 196 (1980).
Westfield Centre Service, Inc. v. Cities Service Oil Co., 172 N.J. Super. 196, 411 A.2d 714, 1980 N.J. Super. LEXIS 453 (App.Div., 1980)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant franchisor challenged the order of the Superior Court, Appellate Division (New Jersey), which affirmed the trial court's order, under N.J. Stat. Ann. §§ 56:10-5 and 56:10-10, that required defendant to pay plaintiff franchisee for the reasonable value of plaintiff's business when defendant chose not to renew plaintiff's franchise for bona fide reasons other than plaintiff's failure to comply with the agreement's requirements.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiff franchisee filed suit against defendant franchisor after defendant informed plaintiff that defendant intended to sell the franchised property, as allowed by a franchise agreement. The trial court held defendant could dispose of its property, so long as it compensated plaintiff for the reasonable value of plaintiff's business as of the date defendant sought to terminate the franchise relationship through nonrenewal. The appellate division and the court affirmed. The court held defendant violated the Franchise Practices Act, N.J. Stat. Ann. § 56:10-1 et seq., as it terminated a franchise without good cause, as defined in N.J. Stat. Ann. § 56:10-5 to include only failure by a franchisee to comply with the franchise agreement. The court held N.J. Stat. Ann. § 56:10-10 entitled plaintiff to recover damages, measured by the reasonable value of his business, and to injunctive relief. The court held the appropriate relief was temporary injunctive relief to maintain the status quo during litigation or to give the franchisee time to move or close the business. The court held § 56:10-10 permitted attorneys' fees on all plaintiff's efforts to obtain an injunction.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment below, which held the Franchise Practices Act (Act) required defendant franchisor to pay plaintiff franchisee damages when defendant chose not to renew plaintiff's franchise, despite plaintiff's compliance with the agreement's requirements. The court held the Act also permitted temporary injunctive relief, as appropriate, and counsel fees.

 

315 of 2470 DOCUMENTS


Knight v. Margate


A-145


Supreme Court of New Jersey


86 N.J. 374; 431 A.2d 833; 1981 N.J. LEXIS 1640


April 6, 1981, Argued  
June 25, 1981, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] On certification to the Superior Court, Law Division.
Knight v. Margate, 85 N.J. 478, 427 A.2d 572, 1980 N.J. LEXIS 1574 (1980)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant state challenged the judgment of the Superior Court of New Jersey, which entered summary judgment in favor of respondent attorneys on the grounds that the statute that prohibited certain public officials and employees, including judges, from casino dealings was unconstitutional.


OVERVIEW: Respondent attorneys, former part-time municipal court judges, brought an action against appellant state and municipalities where they served as magistrates. They alleged that the New Jersey Conflicts of Interest Law, N.J. Stat. Ann. § 52:13D-12 et seq., which prohibited members of the judiciary from casino dealings, did not apply to municipal court judges and alternatively, that it was unconstitutional if construed to apply to any member of the judiciary. The trial court granted summary judgment in favor of respondents. On direct appeal, the court reversed. The court held that the N.J. Const. art. VI, § 1, par. 1 conveyed a broad meaning to the term judiciary to include municipal court judges, and thus the statute applied to them. The court also held that the statute was constitutional. Though the regulation of the judiciary was a power conferred upon the court, it did not preclude the accommodation of lawful and reasonable exercise of the powers of the legislative branch. The statute had a significant governmental purpose of sanitizing casino gambling and its potentially corrupting effect upon government. Further, the statute was consistent with the judicial canons of ethics.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the judgment of the lower court and held that the statute prohibiting casino dealings did apply to municipal judges who were members of the judiciary and that the statute was constitutional because it served an important, legitimate governmental purpose and did not interfere with the court's regulation of the judiciary.

Paul Kimball Hospital, Inc. v. Brick Township Hospital, Inc.


A-58/59


Supreme Court of New Jersey


86 N.J. 429; 432 A.2d 36; 1981 N.J. LEXIS 1643


January 27, 1981, Argued  
June 24, 1981, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] On appeal (A-58-80) from and certification (A-59-80) to the Superior Court, Appellate Division.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellants, a hospital, the health commissioner, and the attorney general, sought review of the decision of the Superior Court, Appellate Division (New Jersey), which held that N.J. Stat. Ann. 26:2H-7 was unconstitutional. The appellate court held that it constituted special legislation to exempt a specific hospital from the certificate of need requirement in contravention of N.J. Const. art. IV, § VII, para. 9(8) and U.S. Const. amend. XIV.


OVERVIEW: Appellant health commissioner denied appellant hospital's request to construct a hospital without a certificate of need. While appellant hospital awaited a hearing the legislature passed N.J. Stat. Ann. 26:2H-7 which exempted the hospital initial certificate of need requirements. Respondent hospital filed an action against appellants and the attorney general intervened as a party defendant and health care facilities intervened as plaintiffs. The trial court found the statute unconstitutional as special legislation in violation of N.J. Const. art. IV, § VII, para. 9(8) and U.S. Const. amend. XIV, and the appellate court affirmed. On appeal, the court reversed the order because the amendment, a grandfather clause, satisfied equal protection criteria. The court held that the fact that only one entity met the specific provisions of the amendment did not render the legislation special. The test was whether any health care facilities were excluded that should have been included. The court found that the classification rested upon a rational basis relevant to the purpose of eliminating of certificates of need for existing hospitals or those which had attained the planning stage.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the appellate court's judgment, which held as unconstitutional a statute which eliminated the requirement that existing hospitals obtain an initial certificate of need requirement to build a health care facility. The court held that the statute was not an impermissible special law and the equal protection criteria were met.

Bd. of Educ. v. Caffiero

Supreme Court of New Jersey


86 N.J. 308; 431 A.2d 799; 1981 N.J. LEXIS 1629


February 10, 1981, Argued  
June 9, 1981, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Appeal dismissed by Caffiero v. Board of Education, 454 U.S. 1025, 102 S. Ct. 560, 70 L. Ed. 2d 470, 1981 U.S. LEXIS 4366 (1981)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] On appeal from the Superior Court, Appellate Division, whose opinion is reported at 173 N.J. Super. 204 (1980).
Board of Education v. Caffiero, 173 N.J. Super. 204, 413 A.2d 981, 1980 N.J. Super. LEXIS 502 (N.J. Super. Ct. App. Div., 1980)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant parents and children appealed from the decision of the Superior Court, Appellate Division (New Jersey), which found that defendant parents were vicariously liable to plaintiff board for damage to schools caused by defendant children.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiff board sought to collect damages from defendant parents for damages their caused by defendant children at a public school. Defendants attacked the constitutionality of N.J. Stat. Ann. § 18A:37-3, which imposed vicarious liability without fault on the parents or guardian of any public school pupil who willfully or maliciously damaged public school property. The court upheld the lower court's rejection of defendants' challenge on due process and equal protection grounds. The court remanded the case with directions for the trial court to reconsider whether plaintiff's summary judgment motion should be granted in light of this opinion.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the appellate court's determination that plaintiff board could hold defendant parents variously liable for damage to a public school caused by defendant children, as the statute permitting such recovery was constitutional. The court remanded the case to the trial court for a determination whether plaintiff's summary judgment motion should be granted, as the trial court had erred in finding the statute to be unconstitutional.

State v. Ryan


A-15


Supreme Court of New Jersey


86 N.J. 1; 429 A.2d 332; 1981 N.J. LEXIS 1610


October 7, 1980, Argued  
April 20, 1981, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] On certification to the Superior Court, Appellate Division, whose opinion is reported at 171 N.J.Super. 427 (1979).


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant challenged his sentence in the trial court (New Jersey) for violation of probation. Appellant alleged that the sentence violated his constitutional rights against double jeopardy.


OVERVIEW: In the hearing on defendant's motion for modification of his sentence in which defendant was granted probation after serving a portion of his prison term, the trial court failed to advise defendant that violation of probation could result in an increased sentence. At defendant's probation violation hearing, the trial court was advised that seven detainers were pending against defendant, that defendant was serving time for a conviction for malicious damage and larceny, and that defendant faced additional punishment for contempt of court. The trial court revoked both the probation and the suspension of the original 3-5 year sentence, imposed a 5-7 year sentence, and refused to grant credit for probation time or prior time served. The court adopted the appellate court's disallowance of sentence credit for time spent on probation and the applicability of N.J. Stat. Ann. § 2A:168-4 for modification of sentence proceedings under N.J. R. Crim. P. 3:21-10(b). The court held that jeopardy attached when defendant began serving his original prison term and that imposition of a longer sentence violated defendant's constitutional rights against double jeopardy.


OUTCOME: The court vacated the sentence imposed on defendant for violation of probation and the original sentence was reinstated. The court held that jeopardy attached when defendant began serving his initial term of incarceration and subsequent imposition of a larger sentence violated defendant's constitutional rights against double jeopardy.

In re Application of Li Volsi


A-39


Supreme Court of New Jersey


85 N.J. 576; 428 A.2d 1268; 1981 N.J. LEXIS 1608; 17 A.L.R.4th 972


October 6, 1980, Argued  
April 13, 1981, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] On a Petition for Review of Rule 1:20A-1, et seq.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner attorney challenged the constitutionality and desirability of the Fee Arbitration Committees established by the Supreme Court of New Jersey in N.J. Ct. R., Code Prof. Conduct DR.1:20A.


OVERVIEW: Petitioner attorney and amicus New Jersey State Bar Association alleged that N.J. Ct. R., Code Prof. Conduct DR1:20A was unconstitutional because the Supreme Court exceeded its authority under N.J. Const. art. VI § II, par. 3, it denied lawyers equal protection of the law, it denied attorneys the right to trial by jury, and the unappealability of Fee Arbitration Committee determinations violated both the due process clause of U.S. Const. amend. XIV and the New Jersey Constitution. The court held that it had the authority to control the substance of attorney fee relationships and of resolving fee disputes. Because lawyers are not a protected "suspect class," the court held that it need only prove a rational basis to assure the reasonableness of attorney-client fees. The court held that the state has a special interest in regulation the legal profession and attorney-client relationships. The court held that the right to a civil trial by jury was not incorporated into U.S. Const. amend. XIV and was not applicable to the states. The court held that there was no constitutional right to an appeal, but that it would review the need for a limited right of appeal.


OUTCOME: The court upheld the constitutionality and validity of its establishment of Fee Arbitration Committees. The court referred the question of amending the rules to provide for a limited right of appeal to the Supreme Court's Civil Practice Committee.

Fid. Union Trust Co. v. N.J. Highway Auth.


A-2


Supreme Court of New Jersey


85 N.J. 277; 426 A.2d 488; 1981 N.J. LEXIS 1591


April 21, 1980, Argued  
March 2, 1981, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Reargued November 3, 1980.


PRIOR HISTORY: On certification to the Superior Court, Chancery Division, whose opinion is reported at 164 N.J. Super. 246 (1978).


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant highway authority sought review of an order of the Superior Court of New Jersey, which declared unconstitutional an amendment to the New Jersey Highway Authority Act, N.J. Stat. Ann. § 27:12B-1 et seq. as an impairment to the obligation of a contract with respondent bondholders.


OVERVIEW: The legislature amended the New Jersey Highway Authority Act, N.J. Stat. Ann. § 27:12B-1 et seq. to allow the governor and either the state treasurer or the comptroller of the treasury to effectively veto toll adjustments. Respondent bondholders sued appellant highway authority and claimed that the amendment impaired the obligation of contracts in violation of the Contracts Clause, U.S. Const. art. I, § 10 and N.J. Const. art. IV, § 7, para. 3. The trial court held that the amendment was unconstitutional. On direct certification, the court found that respondents harbored no reasonable expectation that the composition of appellant was immutable. The court held that the legislature could substitute the officers who administered a project without constitutional violation; that the amendment did not involve a substantial financial condition of the agreement; and that the state had authority to change the structure governing a public entity. The court held that N.J. Const. art. IV, § 7, para. 3 and U.S. Const. art. I, § 10 provided parallel guarantees and that the analysis of contractual impairment was the same under both. Therefore, the court reversed the judgment of the trial court.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the trial court's declaration that an amendment giving the governor and other officials the power to veto toll adjustments was unconstitutional because the amendment was not a substantial impairment to appellant highway authority's contract with respondent bondholders and because the legislature had the authority to make such a structural change.

 

321 of 2470 DOCUMENTS


In re Grady


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


SUPREME COURT OF NEW JERSEY


85 N.J. 235; 426 A.2d 467; 1981 N.J. LEXIS 2590


September 8, 1980, Argued  
February 18, 1981, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY: In re Grady, 170 N.J. Super. 98, 405 A.2d 851, 1979 N.J. Super. LEXIS 878 (N.J. Super. Ct., 1979)



DISPOSITION:  [***1] Judgment vacated, and matter remanded for further proceedings.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellants, a public advocate and the attorney general, challenged the judgment of the Superior Court, Chancery Division (New Jersey), which allowed appellee parents to exercise substituted consent for the sterilization of their mentally impaired daughter.


OVERVIEW: The parents sought to have their mentally incompetent daughter sterilized. The hospital refused to allow the operation without judicially authorized consent. The parents requested such authorization from the trial court. A guardian ad litem was appointed to represent the daughter's interests at the judicial proceedings. The trial court allowed the parents to exercise substituted consent for their daughter to be sterilized. Appellants challenged that judgment. On appeal, the court vacated the judgment and remanded the case for further consideration. The trial court had the inherent power under its parens patriae jurisdiction to decide whether to authorize sterilization for the daughter. Although the court agreed substantially with the trial court's judgment, the court established a stricter standard for determining the question of sterilization and, thus, a remand was necessary. While the trial court applied the clear and convincing standard of proof to its finding of incapacity, it did not apply the clear and convincing standard of proof to its finding that sterilization was in the daughter's best interest.


OUTCOME: The court vacated the judgment that allowed the parents to exercise substituted consent for the sterilization of their mentally impaired daughter and remanded the case for further consideration. Although it rejected the standard advocated by appellants, the court established a stricter standard than that used by the trial court to determine the issue of sterilization.

 

322 of 2470 DOCUMENTS


Valerius v. Newark


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


SUPREME COURT OF NEW JERSEY


84 N.J. 591; 423 A.2d 988; 1980 N.J. LEXIS 2268


September 8, 1980, Argued  
December 23, 1980, Decided



DISPOSITION:  [***1] Reversed and remanded.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellants police officer and his counsel individuals challenged a judgment of the Appellate Division (New Jersey), which reversed a trial court judgment entered in favor of appellants in their action to recover attorney fees from appellee city pursuant to N.J. Stat. Ann. § 40A:14-155.


OVERVIEW: Appellant police officer was indicted for misconduct in office and related offenses. Appellant police officer hired appellant attorney to defend him. Trial was had and appellant police officer was found not guilty of the charged offenses. Appellant attorney submitted to appellee city his bill for legal services rendered. Appellee refused to pay. Appellants filed suit, basing their claim upon N.J. Stat. Ann. § 40A:14-155. The trial court entered a judgment in favor of appellants for almost twice the amount initially billed by appellant attorney. The lower appellate court reversed, holding that the indictment against appellant police officer arose out of the pervasion and prosecution of his duties, and that the fact of his acquittal was immaterial. On appeal, the court reversed. The fact that appellant officer was acquitted of all charges brought the indictment within the terms of § 40A:14-155. Nevertheless, appellants were entitled only to the amount initially billed by appellant attorney.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the lower appellate court's judgment in favor of appellee city and remanded; appellants police officer and attorney were entitled to recover the amount billed by appellant attorney for legal services rendered to appellant police officer in connection with charges that arose out of appellant police officer's official duties.

State v. Maguire


A-149


Supreme Court of New Jersey


84 N.J. 508; 423 A.2d 294; 1980 N.J. LEXIS 1402


March 4, 1980, Argued  
December 2, 1980, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] On certification to Superior Court, Law Division, Resentencing Panel, whose opinion is reported at    N.J. Super.     (1980).
State v. Maguire, 176 N.J. Super. 164, 422 A.2d 466, 1979 N.J. Super. LEXIS 1032 (N.J. Super. Ct., 1979)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The state challenged the decision from the Superior Court, Law Division, Resentencing Panel (New Jersey), which granted defendant's petition for a sentence review.


OVERVIEW: Defendant petitioned for sentence review. defendant argued that without further proof of at least one of the sentence enhancement criteria in N.J. Stat. Ann. 2C:44-3, the maximum sentence for murder was the 30-year term provided in N.J. Stat. Ann. 2C:11-3(b) and that even if the penalty of life imprisonment was currently a discretionary sentencing option for murder under the Code, his mandatory life sentence should be deemed to "exceed" a discretionary maximum sentence of life imprisonment. The resentencing panel determined that defendant was eligible for a sentence reconsideration hearing under N.J. Stat. Ann. 2C:1-1(d)(2). The court granted direct certification. The court reversed the decision of the resentencing panel because the court held that no one now serving a mandatory life sentence for murder under former law was entitled to reconsideration of that sentence under the resentencing provisions of the Code, and that the crime of murder under the Code carried a potential life sentence without the necessity of proving an enhancement criterion.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the decision of the resentencing panel because the court held that defendant was not entitled to reconsideration of his sentence under the resentencing provisions of the Code, and that the crime of murder carried a potential life sentence without the necessity of proving an enhancement criterion.

 

324 of 2470 DOCUMENTS


State v. Schmid


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


SUPREME COURT OF NEW JERSEY


84 N.J. 535; 423 A.2d 615; 1980 N.J. LEXIS 2265


February 4, 1980, Argued  
November 25, 1980, Decided


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant, a political party member, appealed an order of the Superior Court (New Jersey), which, following a trial de novo, found defendant guilty of trespass under N.J. Stat. Ann. § 2A:170-31.


OVERVIEW: Defendant, a political party member, was arrested by campus police for trespass upon private property after police found defendant distributing political literature on campus in violation of campus regulations. Defendant was convicted of trespass, pursuant to N.J. Stat. Ann. § 2A:170-31. Following a trial de novo, the intermediate court found defendant guilty and imposed a monetary penalty. On further appeal, defendant contended that the conviction illegally stemmed from a violation of federal and state constitutional rights to freedom of speech and assembly. While the appellate court found no state action to justify defendant's argument under U.S. Const. amend. I, it reversed defendant's conviction because it found that the university violated defendant's state constitutional rights of expression. The court determined that the university regulations that were applied to defendant contained no standards, aside from the requirement for invitation and permission, for governing the actual exercise of expressional freedom. The court held that regulations thus devoid of reasonable standards could not constitutionally be invoked to prohibit defendant's reasonable exercise of his rights.


OUTCOME: The appellate court reversed an order of the intermediate court that, upon trial de novo, found defendant, a political party member, guilty of trespass. The court held that the conviction illegally stemmed from a violation of state constitutional rights to freedom of speech and assembly.

 

325 of 2470 DOCUMENTS


State v. Barnes


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


SUPREME COURT OF NEW JERSEY


84 N.J. 362; 420 A.2d 303; 1980 N.J. LEXIS 2267


January 22, 1980, Argued  
September 22, 1980, Decided



DISPOSITION:  [***1] Reversed and remanded.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The state sought review of the order from the Superior Court of New Jersey, Appellate Division, that dismissed the prosecution's appeal and sustained the trial court's judgments of acquittal and finding that the municipal ordinance that regulated peddlers on the city streets was unconstitutionally vague and overbroad.


OVERVIEW: Defendants were charged with 116 violations of a municipal ordinance regulating peddlers on the city streets. In municipal court, one defendant was found guilty and other defendants guilty pleas to the remaining 115 complaints. All defendants appealed to the county court, which found the ordinance under which the defendants were convicted unconstitutionally vague and overbroad on its face and entered a judgment reversing the convictions and acquitting defendants. The state appealed the constitutional question to the appellate division court, which dismissed the appeal. The instant court granted the state's petition for certification to address the issue of whether the appeal was precluded by either court rules or the double jeopardy clauses of the federal and state constitutions. The instant court ruled that the appellate division court should not have dismissed the prosecution's appeal. A successful appeal by the state would not subject defendants to successive prosecutions or multiple punishments but only reinstatement of their guilty pleas or reinstatement of defendant's conviction following his trial. There were no double jeopardy issues involved in the cases.


OUTCOME: The instant court reversed the appellate division court's order that denied the prosecution the right of appeal because the prosecution was entitled to appeal the constitutional issue raised in the trial court without offending established principles of double jeopardy. The cause was remanded to the lower court for consideration of the constitutionality of the ordinance.

Matthews v. Atlantic City


A-196


Supreme Court of New Jersey


84 N.J. 153; 417 A.2d 1011; 1980 N.J. LEXIS 1392


April 29, 1980, Argued  
July 30, 1980, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] On certification to the Superior Court, Appellate Division.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant candidate sought review of the decision of the Superior Court, Appellate Division (New Jersey), which affirmed the trial court determination that the two-year residency requirement to become a member of the board of commissioners, contained in N.J. Stat. Ann. § 40:72-1 was constitutional.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiff candidate sought to be elected to the board of commissioners of Atlantic City. N.J. Stat. Ann. § 40:72-1, part of the Walsh Act, had a two-year residency requirement for the period prior to the election. When plaintiff filed for the election, he also contested the constitutionality of the statute. The trial court found the statute to be constitutional and the appellate court affirmed. The court found that the Walsh Act applied to only a small number of municipal governments that chose to operate under the commission form of government. As such, the residency requirement only affected these few municipalities. The court reversed the lower court, holding that the residency requirement violated the Equal Protection Clause of U.S. Const. amend. XIV, because it substantially infringed on a voter's right to exercise his franchise.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the determination that the two-year residency requirement to become a member of the board of commissioners was constitutional, holding that it was a substantial infringement on a voter's right to exercise his franchise, contrary to the Equal Protection Clause of the U.S. Constitution, allowing plaintiff candidate to pursue that elected office.

Pierce v. Ortho Pharmaceutical Corp.


A-51


Supreme Court of New Jersey


84 N.J. 58; 417 A.2d 505; 1980 N.J. LEXIS 1394; 12 A.L.R.4th 520; 115 L.R.R.M. 3044; 1 I.E.R. Cas. (BNA) 109; 101 Lab. Cas. (CCH) P55,477


November 13, 1979, Argued  
July 28, 1980, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] On certification to the Superior Court, Appellate Division, whose opinion is reported at 166 N.J.Super. 335 (1979).


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant drug manufacturer filed a petition for certification to the Superior Court, Appellate Division (New Jersey), seeking review of that court's decision, which reversed the trial court's grant of summary judgment in defendant's favor on plaintiff medical doctor's wrongful discharge claims and remanded the case for a full trial.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiff, a medical doctor, worked for defendant, a drug manufacturer. Plaintiff opposed the development of a drug containing saccharin. In plaintiff's opinion, the saccharin's safety was medically debatable. Defendant removed plaintiff from her work on the drug project but told her that she could choose other projects. Plaintiff tendered her resignation, which defendant accepted. Plaintiff sued for damages for the termination of her employment. The trial court rejected defendant's argument that plaintiff's suit was barred because she resigned. There was a fact question of whether her resignation was forced. The trial court, however, granted defendant's motion for summary judgment because plaintiff was an employee at will and could be terminated at any time. The intermediary appellate court reversed and remanded. Defendant sought further review. The supreme court noted that plaintiff did not allege that saccharin was dangerous, but that it was controversial. Also, plaintiff's research did not violate medical ethics or any statute. There was no clear mandate of public policy that prevented plaintiff from working on the drug, and thus defendant was entitled to summary judgment.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the appellate court's decision and remanded the cause to the trial court for entry of judgment in favor of defendant because plaintiff's termination from her work was not for reasons that were contrary to a clear mandate of public policy.

State v. Sugar


A-190


Supreme Court of New Jersey


84 N.J. 1; 417 A.2d 474; 1980 N.J. LEXIS 1389


May 6, 1980, Argued  
July 24, 1980, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] On certification to the Superior Court, Law Division.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The state challenged by certification the order from the Superior Court, Law Division that ordered dismissal of the charges pending against respondent for murder of his wife because of intentional eavesdropping by law enforcement officers of conversations between respondent and his attorney.


OVERVIEW: Respondent was arrested for the disappearance of his wife, advised of his custodial rights as required by Miranda, and taken to police headquarters. Respondent asked to consult with his attorney. Law enforcement officers intentionally eavesdropped on the conversations between respondent and his attorney and then charged respondent with murder. Respondent's attorney suspected that their conversations had been overheard when police seemed to know exactly where to search respondent's home. The trial court dismissed the charges against respondent and found that the eavesdropping denied respondent the effective assistance of counsel in violation of U. S. Const. amend. VI and N.J. Const. art. I § 10. The court expressed its outrage at the actions of the police officers. The court held that, because respondent did not claim that his confidence in his attorney was impaired and it did not appear that trial strategy was disclosed, dismissal was not required. Before indictment, the court required the state to establish beyond a reasonable doubt that it could conduct a prosecution with unsullied evidence and witnesses and that no tainted witnesses or evidence would be produced.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the judgment of the trial court and the appellate court and remanded for further proceedings. The court enjoined the state from conducting any grand jury proceedings until the trial court ruled on the suppression of tainted evidence and witnesses from grand jury deliberations.

Mt. Laurel v. Department of Public Advocate


A-157


Supreme Court of New Jersey


83 N.J. 522; 416 A.2d 886; 1980 N.J. LEXIS 1375


April 22, 1980, Argued  
July 21, 1980, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] On certification to the Superior Court, Law Division, and Department of the Public Advocate.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff township sought review of an order of the Superior Court of New Jersey, which dismissed plaintiff's claims against defendant public advocate for reimbursement of legal fees in a case where defendant represented plaintiff's adversaries.


OVERVIEW: Defendant public advocate represented the adversaries of plaintiff township in a case to force compliance with a court order to modify plaintiff's exclusionary zoning ordinance. Defendant denied plaintiff's request to appoint outside counsel and rejected plaintiff's claim for legal expenses. The trial court dismissed plaintiff's claims and the court ordered direct certification of plaintiff's appeals. The court held that the Department of the Public Advocate Act of 1974 (Act), N.J. Stat. Ann. § 52:27E-1 to -47, did not violate the separation of powers clause, N.J. Const. art. III, para. 1, and was a valid delegation of its authority to a governmental agency. The court held that the Act did not violate N.J. Const. art. VIII, § 3, para. 3 because the mere fact that a public expenditure benefitted a private party did not render the expenditure unconstitutional. The court held that defendant's decision denying reimbursement to plaintiffs was well reasoned, based upon sufficient information and knowledge of the issue involved, and clearly within his discretion. Therefore, the court affirmed the decision of the trial court.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the trial court's dismissal of plaintiff township's claims against defendant public advocate for legal fees in a case where defendant represented plaintiff's adversaries because defendant's decision that plaintiff's position was not in the public interest was within his discretion.

Quick Chek Food Stores v. Springfield


A-111


Supreme Court of New Jersey


83 N.J. 438; 416 A.2d 840; 1980 N.J. LEXIS 1380


March 3, 1980, Argued  
July 14, 1980, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] On certification to the Superior Court, Appellate Division.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff corporation appealed the judgment from the Superior Court of New Jersey, which affirmed the trial court's declaratory judgment that an ordinance passed by defendant township to mandate closing hours for certain businesses was constitutional.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiff corporation owned and operated a chain of convenience food outlets that were open until midnight. Defendant township adopted an ordinance to limit the hours of operation of businesses in neighborhood-commercial zones. The trial court and appellate court rejected plaintiff's constitutionality challenge of the ordinance. The court first affirmed the denial of plaintiff's motion for a new trial to produce additional evidence under N.J. R. Civ. P. 4:49-1 because plaintiff offered no reason for its failure to produce the evidence at the time of trial. The court also affirmed that defendant had authority under its police power to make ordinances for the public health and welfare, pursuant to N.J. Stat. Ann. § 40:48-2. N.J. Const. art. IV, § 7 (1947) provided that any such ordinances should be liberally construed in favor of the municipality. Accordingly, the court held that defendant had the authority to restrict business hours because the ordinance was reasonably related to a legitimate object of the public health and welfare of the residential community. Plaintiff failed in its burden to clearly demonstrate that the ordinance was arbitrary and unreasonable.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the declaratory judgment that an ordinance passed by defendant township was constitutional in mandating the closing hours for plaintiff corporation's businesses. Defendant had power under its police power to make ordinances for the public health and welfare, absent clear evidence by plaintiff that the ordinance was arbitrary and unreasonable.

 

331 of 2470 DOCUMENTS


State v. Miller


A-27


Supreme Court of New Jersey


83 N.J. 402; 416 A.2d 821; 1980 N.J. LEXIS 1367


October 23, 1979, Argued  
July 8, 1980, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] On appeal from the Appellate Division, Superior Court, whose opinion is reported at 162 N.J.Super. 333 (1978).


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant Borough of Milltown sought review of the decision of the Appellate Division, Superior Court (New Jersey), which reversed the conviction of respondent homeowner, who had posted a political sign in his yard, holding that the municipal ordinance regulating signs violated the U.S. Const. amend. I by absolutely prohibiting political and public interest expression and was unconstitutional as applied to respondent's sign.


OVERVIEW: Respondent homeowner was convicted of violating Borough of Milltown, N.J., Zoning Ordinance § 20-9.1(a), by erecting a sign in his front yard. The Appellate Division, Superior Court, reversed the conviction, holding that the ordinance was an unconstitutional restriction on respondent's freedom of speech. The court found that a community had the right to regulate signs and other structures to maintain the aesthetics of the area. The court found that an ordinance which restricted U.S. Const. amend. I political speech would be given greater scrutiny than an ordinance regulating other types of speech. The court found that the sign restriction had to be tied to a compelling municipal interest as well as to the uses permitted in an area. Applying this standard, the court held that the constitutionality of the ordinance here could not be sustained. The court affirmed, holding that adequate alternative means of political communication were not available to owners who are precluded from putting signs and posters in their yards, so the ordinance was unconstitutional on its face.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the reversal of respondent homeowner's conviction for violating a sign ordinance, and declaration that appellant Borough of Milltown's ordinance was unconstitutional, holding that respondent's right to political speech under the First Amendment was violated by the ordinance.

Jersey Shore Medical Center-Fitkin Hospital v. Estate of Baum


A-132


Supreme Court of New Jersey


84 N.J. 137; 417 A.2d 1003; 1980 N.J. LEXIS 1395; 11 A.L.R.4th 1147


March 3, 1980, Argued  
July 2, 1980, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] On certification to the Superior Court, Law Division.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff hospital appealed a judgment of the trial court (New Jersey) that granted summary judgment for defendant wife and dismissed plaintiff's suit that sought payment of medical expenses incurred by defendant's deceased husband during his last illness.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiff hospital sued defendant wife to collect medical expenses incurred by the decedent husband during his last illness. The trial court granted summary judgment for defendant and plaintiff appealed. The appeals court affirmed, holding that plaintiff, a judgment creditor, had to first seek satisfaction from the income and other property of decedent, who incurred the debt. If the decedent's financial resources were insufficient, plaintiff could then seek satisfaction from the income and property of defendant. The court found that the common law rule concerning necessaries, with its inherent discrimination against husbands, constituted a denial of equal protection of the laws under the state constitution. However, in the absence of an agreement that defendant would pay for the care of decedent, plaintiff could only seek payment from defendant after it was determined that the financial resources of decedent were insufficient. There was no basis to hold defendant liable for her husband's obligation to plaintiff.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed a judgment of the trial court that granted summary judgment for defendant wife and dismissed plaintiff hospital's suit that sought payment of medical expenses incurred by defendant's deceased husband. The court held that although the common law rule concerning necessaries constituted a denial of equal protection of the laws, plaintiff had to first seek satisfaction from decedent, who incurred the debt.

Rubin v. Glaser


A-71


Supreme Court of New Jersey


83 N.J. 299; 416 A.2d 382; 1980 N.J. LEXIS 1361


February 19, 1980, Argued  
June 19, 1980, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] On appeal from the Superior Court, Appellate Division, whose opinion is reported at 166 N.J.Super. 258 (1979).


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant homeowner sought review of a decision by the Superior Court, Appellate Division (New Jersey), which denied his constitutional challenge to the Homestead Rebate Act, N.J. Stat. Ann. § 54:4-3.80 et seq. Appellant paid a property tax on a vacation home he owned on the Jersey shore but was unable to take advantage of a rebated paid by respondent Director of the Division of Taxation because he lived out of state.


OVERVIEW: Appellant homeowner paid property taxes on a vacation home he owned on the Jersey shore but was unable to take advantage of a tax rebate paid by respondent Director of the Division of Taxation under the Homestead Rebate Act, N.J. Stat. Ann. § 54:4-3.80 et seq. because he lived out-of-state. Appellant challenged the constitutionality of the statute. The court affirmed the decision of the lower court and found that the statute was constitutional. The court held that the state legislature did not exceed its authority under N.J. Const. art. VIII, § 1, par. 5 in enacting the law. The court found that it did not violated the Privileges and Immunities Clause, U.S. Const. art. IV, § 2, cl. 1, because the purpose of the statute was to promote home ownership by assisting taxpayers with large property tax assessments. The court found that the statute was not designed to subsidize vacation homes whether they were owned by in-state or out-of-state residents. Finally, the court found that the denial of a rebate on a vacation home did not rise to the level of a deprivation of a basic necessity of life or of a fundamental right protected by U.S. Const. amend. XIV.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the decision of the lower court and held that the Homestead Rebate Act was constitutional. The court found that the statute did not unconstitutionally disadvantage appellant homeowner because he was an out-of-state resident and ineligible to receive a rebate from respondent Director of the Division of Taxation.

Velmohos v. Maren Engineering Corp.

Supreme Court of New Jersey

June 18, 1980, Decided

PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] On appeal from the Superior Court, Appellate Division, whose opinion is reported at 168 N.J. Super. 520 (1979).

Miller v. New Jersey Ins. Underwriting Asso.


A-73


Supreme Court of New Jersey


82 N.J. 594; 414 A.2d 1322; 1980 N.J. LEXIS 1350


January 7, 1980, Argued  
June 3, 1980, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] On appeal from the Superior Court, Appellate Division.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff mortgagees sought consolidated review of decisions by the Superior Court, Appellate Division (New Jersey), granting summary judgment to defendant insurer and dismissing plaintiffs' claims for payment, under fire insurance policies issued by defendant, for damages to real properties upon which a municipality had obtained in rem judgments of foreclosure for unpaid property taxes.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiff mortgagees challenged the appeals court's dismissal on summary judgment of plaintiffs' claims against defendant insurer for fire damages to real properties on which the city had foreclosed due to nonpayment of real estate taxes. Defendant's policies named the property owners as insureds and plaintiffs as mortgagees. Upon review, the court held that plaintiffs retained insurable interests in their respective properties although the city held title through foreclosure. Plaintiffs' insurable interests were not required to rise to the level of legal or equitable title as long as plaintiffs had a reasonable expectation that the fire policies would reimburse them in case of fire loss. Both plaintiffs continued to occupy and operate their businesses from the insured premises after the city foreclosed. Plaintiffs' continuing possession left them with insurable interests of value. The court reversed and remanded for trial.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the appeals court's grant of summary judgment to defendant insurer because plaintiff mortgagees had insurable interests in business premises they continued to occupy after the city foreclosed on the properties for failure to pay real estate taxes. Defendant was required to pay plaintiffs for damages pursuant to fire insurance policies defendant issued naming plaintiffs as mortgagees. The court remanded for trial.

In re Health Care Administration Board


A-83/84


Supreme Court of New Jersey


83 N.J. 67; 415 A.2d 1147; 1980 N.J. LEXIS 1365


March 4, 1980, Argued  
May 28, 1980, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Writ of certiorari denied and appeal dismissed Wayne Haven Nursing Home v. Finley, 449 U.S. 944, 101 S. Ct. 342, 66 L. Ed. 2d 208, 1980 U.S. LEXIS 3668 (1980)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] On certification to the Superior Court, Appellate Division, whose opinion is reported at 168 N.J. Super. 152 (1979).
New Jersey Asso. of Health Care Facilities v. Finley, 168 N.J. Super. 152, 402 A.2d 246, 1979 N.J. Super. LEXIS 757 (App.Div., 1979)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant nursing homes sought review from the Superior Court, Appellate Division (New Jersey), which upheld appellee health commissioner's approval of regulations which required appellants to make available "a reasonable number of their beds to indigent persons" as a condition of licensure. Appellants argued that the regulations constituted a taking, the language of the regulations was unconstitutionally vague, and equal protection concerns.


OVERVIEW: Appellee health commissioner's approved regulations required appellant nursing homes to make available "a reasonable number of their beds to indigent persons" as a condition of licensure. Appellants objected to the regulations but the lower court upheld implementation of the regulations. Appellants sought review and argued that the regulations constituted a taking of private property for public use without just compensation, the language of the regulations was unconstitutionally vague, and violated equal protection. On review, the court found that the regulations were directed at a social problem affecting the health and welfare of the needy, aged, and infirm, and appellee was well within the agency power granted by the legislature. Also, this did not constitute a taking of private property without just compensation. The court found that the terminology challenged by appellants was definite enough to satisfy the requirements of due process and were not impermissibly vague. The court observed that the regulations applied to both private and public health care facilities and that appellants failed to show lack of a rational basis for the regulations. The decision was affirmed.


OUTCOME: The court upheld appellee health commissioner's approval of the regulations which required appellant nursing homes to make available "a reasonable number of their beds to indigent persons" as a condition of licensure. This was because appellee had the power to implement the regulations, which were not impermissibly vague, and the regulations did not violate appellants' constitutional due process or equal protection rights.

 

339 of 2470 DOCUMENTS


State v. Carpentieri


A-122


Supreme Court of New Jersey


82 N.J. 546; 414 A.2d 966; 1980 N.J. LEXIS 1342


February 5, 1980, Argued  
May 19, 1980, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] On certification to the Superior Court, Appellate Division, whose opinion is reported at 168 N.J.Super. 589 (1979).


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The State appealed a judgment of the Superior Court, Appellate Division (New Jersey), which reversed defendant's convictions for possession of marijuana in violation of N.J. Stat. Ann. § 24:21-20(a)(4), and possession with intent to distribute in violation of N.J. Stat. Ann. § 24:21-19(a)(1), on the ground that the trial court erred in denying defendant's motion to suppress evidence seized during a random traffic stop.


OVERVIEW: While defendant's appeal from his convictions for possession of marijuana and possession with intent to distribute was pending, the United State Supreme Court held that random traffic stops were constitutionally invalid. The appellate court gave retroactive application to that holding and reversed defendant's convictions because the trial court refused to suppress evidence that was seized from defendant's car after it was stopped for a routine license and registration check. The State appealed, and the court reversed the appellate court's judgment because the new rule of law applied only to random traffic stops that occurred after the date of the United States Supreme Court's decision. The court determined that illegal searches would not be deterred by retroactive application of the new rule to law enforcement actions that were undertaken in good-faith reliance upon previous long-standing legal authority. The court rejected the argument that summary orders by the United States Supreme Court directing remand for reconsideration in light of new exclusionary rule decisions mandated retroactive application of such new decisions.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the judgment that reversed defendant's convictions. The court held that the rule that random stops for credential checks were constitutionally invalid could not be retroactively applied to random stops that occurred before that rule, which was a radical departure from state law, was announced by the United States Supreme Court. The court remanded the case for consideration of defendant's remaining contention for reversal.

 

340 of 2470 DOCUMENTS


Kruvant v. Cedar Grove


A-67


Supreme Court of New Jersey


82 N.J. 435; 414 A.2d 9; 1980 N.J. LEXIS 1340


January 8, 1980, Argued  
May 12, 1980, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] On appeal from the Superior Court, Appellate Division.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant construction company sought review of a judgment of the Superior Court of New Jersey that held that appellants were entitled to a variance to construct an apartment complex, but that the trial court erred when it invalidated respondent township's zoning ordinance and held that appellants' variance request was controlled by the ordinance which was operative when the variance was sought.


OVERVIEW: Appellant construction company challenged the appellate court's judgment that held that appellants had been entitled to a zoning variance to construct an apartment complex, but that the trial court erred when it struck down respondent township's zoning ordinance, and held that appellant's variance was controlled by the ordinance that was operative when the variance was sought. The court modified and found that after eight years of protracted litigation, it was proper that the trial court gave respondent 120 days to amend its zoning ordinance to permit multiple dwelling use on appellant's property. The court noted the trial court's frustration with respondent's continuous efforts to rezone the property so as to have permitted the most restrictive use of the property. The court found that respondent's amended restricted commercial zone was identical to the initial zoning category in that neither permitted a feasible use of appellant's land. The court held that under the initial ordinance, appellants were entitled to a variance. The court found that appellant's construction would not have impaired the intent or purpose of respondent's zoning plan under N.J. Stat. Ann. § 40:55D-70.


OUTCOME: A judgment that found that appellant construction company was entitled to a zoning variance and that the trial court erred when it struck down respondent township's zoning ordinance was modified because appellants were entitled to a variance under the original ordinance, because an amended ordinance was too restrictive to permit feasible use of appellant's land, and because appellant's construction did not impair respondent's zoning plan.

 

341 of 2470 DOCUMENTS


Salorio v. Glaser


A-60


Supreme Court of New Jersey


82 N.J. 482; 414 A.2d 943; 1980 N.J. LEXIS 1347


November 14, 1979, Argued  
March 26, 1980, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] On certification to the Superior Court, Chancery Division.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiffs, residents of New York who commuted to New Jersey for work, challenged the adverse judgment of the New Jersey Superior Court, in their suit challenging the constitutionality of N.J. Stat. Ann. § 54:8A-1, which imposed a transportation tax solely upon non-residents. Plaintiffs argued that the tax constituted an equal protection and a privileges and immunity violation.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiffs, New York residents who commuted to work in New Jersey, brought suit challenging the constitutionality of N.J. Stat. Ann. § 54:8A-1, which imposed an emergency transportation tax solely upon New York residents, claiming impermissible discrimination. After the trial court deemed the tax constitutional, plaintiffs appealed the adverse judgment. In affirming in part, the court concluded that there was no equal protection violation. The alleviation of a transportation emergency was a legitimate government purpose, and plaintiffs failed to meet their burden of demonstrating that the imposition of the tax upon New York residents lacked any rational basis. In remanding the matter as to the privileges and immunities claim, however, the court concluded that because the record was insufficient to establish that non-residents were taxed only to the extent of their contribution to the transportation problem, it was unable to apply the correct standard of substantial equality. Remand was necessary to permit a full examination of the benefits and burdens to non-resident commuters occasioned by the transportation programs and imposition of the tax.


OUTCOME: The judgment holding the transportation tax constitutional was affirmed in part, as the tax did not constitute an equal protection violation to plaintiff New York residents, where there was no showing that the imposition of the tax upon non-residents lacked a rational basis. The court was unable to decide privileges and immunities claim based upon the insufficient record and remanded the matter for a full exploration.

 

342 of 2470 DOCUMENTS


Plainfield v. Public Serv. Elec. & Gas Co.


A-21


Supreme Court of New Jersey


82 N.J. 245; 412 A.2d 759; 1980 N.J. LEXIS 1333


October 9, 1979, Argued  
March 10, 1980, Decided; June 5, 1980, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Rehearing Granted April 21, 1980.


PRIOR HISTORY: On certification to Superior Court of New Jersey, Appellate Division.
Plainfield v. Public Service Electric & Gas Co., 79 N.J. 487, 401 A.2d 243, 1979 N.J. LEXIS 2180 (1979)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant utility sought review of a judgment from the Superior Court, Appellate Division (New Jersey). The appellate court upheld the res judicata effect of a 1916 decision, which held that the 1911 Public Utility Act, N.J. Stat. Ann. §§ 48:3-1 and 48:3-4, did not invalidate a contract by appellant's predecessor and respondent city for free lighting service.


OVERVIEW: Respondent city contracted with the predecessor of appellant utility in 1898 to receive free lighting service for municipal buildings. In 1916, an appeals court held that the 1911 Public Utility Act, N.J. Stat. Ann. §§ 48:3-1 and 48:3-4, which prohibited unjustly discriminatory or preferential rates, did not invalidate the contract. In the 1970s, respondent sought declaratory relief to determine whether appellant had to provide free lighting service for three new city buildings. The trial court ruled in favor of respondent, and the appellate court affirmed. The court reversed. The 1916 appeals court decision was not res judicata because the issue was one purely of law; a new adjudication was necessary to avoid inequity. The 1911 Public Utility Act had to be applied uniformly to all rates, including those set prior to the statute's enactment. Applying the act to rates set by the 1898 contract did not violate the contract clauses of U.S. Const. art. I, § 10, cl. 1, or N.J. Const. art. 4, § 7, par. 3 (1947). Where a state enacted legislation that abrogated a contract entered into by a municipality, the municipality could not seek protection under the constitutional contract clauses.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the judgment that an 1898 contract between respondent city and appellant utility's predecessor, which gave respondent free lighting service, was still enforceable. The 1911 Public Utility Act, which barred preferential rates, applied to invalidate the rates set by the contract. Respondent, as a municipal corporation, was not protected by federal or state constitutional contract clauses.

 

343 of 2470 DOCUMENTS


Heir v. Degnan


A-102/103


Supreme Court of New Jersey


82 N.J. 109; 411 A.2d 194; 1980 N.J. LEXIS 1315


December 10, 1979, Argued  
February 11, 1980, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] On certification to the Division of Alcoholic Beverage Control, Department of Public Safety, State of New Jersey.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: New Jersey division of alcoholic beverage control (ABC) certified appeals attacking the validity of regulations, adopted by its director, which eliminated retail price maintenance in the alcoholic beverage industry, except for sales below cost, and which modified significantly the previous policy of the ABC regarding price controls and competition.


OVERVIEW: Two appeals were filed attacking new regulations adopted by the state division of alcoholic beverage control (ABC), one on behalf of liquor retailers and the other on behalf of wholesale solicitors. Both appeals challenged the power of the ABC to make certain changes in the absence of specific legislative authorization. Appellants alleged that the procedures utilized in adopting the new regulations violated due process. The specific regulations attacked included those which: (1) authorized quantity discounts, (2) regulated credit practices, (3) required purchases from state wholesalers, (4) banned cooperative advertising, (5) prohibited sales below cost, and (6) provided emergency measures for sales tax collection. The court concluded that elimination of retail price maintenance and allowance of price competition was within the powers granted to the ABC director by the legislature. The court ruled that the procedures employed in adopting the regulations satisfied due process and that the challenges to specific regulations were without merit, except for those prohibiting non-price cooperative advertising and providing emergency measures for sales tax collection.


OUTCOME: The court upheld the action of the state division of alcoholic beverage control in adopting regulations as a permissible delegation of authority; specific regulations attacked by appellants were upheld, except for the ban on non-price cooperative advertising and imposition of emergency tax collection measures.

New Jersey State Chamber of Commerce v. New Jersey Election Law Enforcement Com.


A-83


Supreme Court of New Jersey


82 N.J. 57; 411 A.2d 168; 1980 N.J. LEXIS 1316


May 9, 1979, Argued  
February 6, 1980, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Order May 8, 1980.


PRIOR HISTORY: On appeal from the Superior Court, Appellate Division, whose opinion is reported at 155 N.J. Super. 218 (1977).


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiffs appealed from a judgment by which the Superior Court, Appellate Division, (Pennsylvania) upheld the constitutionality of parts of the New Jersey Campaign Contributions and Expenditures Reporting Act, N.J. Stat. Ann. §§ 19:44A-1 - 26, against a challenge under U.S. Const. amend. I. Plaintiffs claimed that the provisions regulating the efforts of political information organizations to influence legislation were facially overboard.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiffs challenged the constitutionality of the New Jersey Campaign Contributions and Expenditures Reporting Act (Act), N.J. Stat. Ann. §§ 19:44A-1 to 26, contending that those portions regulating the efforts of political information organizations to influence legislation were facially overbroad in violation of U.S. Const. amend. I. The trial court held the provisions invalid, and the intermediate court reversed. Using "judicial surgery" to effect a narrow construction of the challenged provisions, the court affirmed the intermediate court's judgment as modified. The court interpreted the phrase "to influence legislation" to mean activity consisting of direct, express, and intentional communications with legislators undertaken on a substantial basis by individuals acting jointly for the specific purpose of seeking to affect the introduction, passage, or defeat of, or to affect the content of legislative proposals. So construed, the court held, the Act was not facially overbroad. The court also set aside N.J. Admin. Code tit. 19, § 12.1(e), an administrative regulation imposing a monetary threshold for enforcement of the Act's provisions, on grounds that the amount was too low.


OUTCOME: Holding that the challenged provisions of the New Jersey Campaign Contributions and Expenditures Reporting Act, as narrowly construed, withstood constitutional challenge on First Amendment grounds, the court affirmed the intermediate court's judgment as modified.

Camden v. Byrne


A-5/6/7/8/9/10/80


Supreme Court of New Jersey


82 N.J. 133; 411 A.2d 462; 1980 N.J. LEXIS 1317


September 10, 1979, Argued  
February 5, 1980, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] On certification to the Superior Court, Appellate Division (A-5/6/7/8/9/80 Camden v. Byrne). 

On certification to the Superior Court, Appellate Division, whose opinion is reported at 159 N.J. Super. 297 (1978) (A-10 Smith v. Goldman). 
Smith v. Goldman, 159 N.J. Super. 297, 387 A.2d 1239, 1978 N.J. Super. LEXIS 910 (App.Div., 1978)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Numerous parties, municipalities and their officials, appealed from decisions of the Superior Court (New Jersey) seeking certification and raising identical issues. Several municipalities and counties challenged the failure of the Governor and legislature and certain state officials to appropriate and expend state revenues pursuant to particular statutes which purported to provide funds for local government uses.


OVERVIEW: Appellant municipalities initiated litigation calling for disbursements of tax revenues, namely, the sales and use tax, N.J. Stat. Ann. § 54:32B-31, the transfer inheritance tax, N.J. Stat. Ann. § 54:33-10, and the bus franchise replacement tax, N.J. Stat. Ann. § 48:4-14.2, as well as for the expenditure of state funds for county highways, N.J. Stat. Ann. § 52:27B-20 B(2)(a), (b) and (c) and N.J. Stat. Ann. § 27:14-1. The court granted review of the several cases from the appeals courts. Several municipalities challenged the failure of the Governor, legislature and certain officials to appropriate and expend state revenues pursuant to particular statute, which purported to provide funds for local government uses. The challenges presented the court with questions concerning the appropriate constitutional framework governing the state budgetary process and the proper role of the courts in resolving conflicting claims between the state and governmental subdivisions over entitlement to state revenues. The court concluded the relief sought by the appellants could only be accomplished if there was a valid legislative appropriation. The lower court judgments were affirmed.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the lower court judgments against the appellants. The court concluded that it was not the proper role of the courts to resolve conflicting claims between the state and its governmental subdivisions over entitlement to state revenues, where the state constitution stated the legislature made such appropriations.

Cold Indian Springs Corp. v. Ocean


A 40/41/42/121


Supreme Court of New Jersey


81 N.J. 502; 410 A.2d 652; 1980 N.J. LEXIS 1307


October 23, 1979, Argued  
January 24, 1980, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] On appeal from the Superior Court, Appellate Division, whose opinion is reported at 161 N.J. Super. (1978).
Cold Indian Springs Corp. v. Ocean, 161 N.J. Super. 586, 392 A.2d 175, 1978 N.J. Super. LEXIS 1007 (App.Div., 1978)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant rental property owners challenged a judgment, in consolidated appeals, from the Superior Court, Appellate Division (New Jersey) that upheld the constitutionality of the Tenants' Property Tax Rebate Act, N.J. Stat. Ann. § 54:4-6.2 et seq. Appellants asserted that the provision of the act that included municipal-wide revaluation of property, but excluded property tax judgments when calculating reductions, was unconstitutional.


OVERVIEW: In consolidated appeals, the court affirmed a judgment that upheld the constitutionality of the Tenants' Property Tax Rebate Act (the act), N.J. Stat. Ann. § 54:4-6.2 et seq. The act, recognizing that rent levels anticipate the tax burden on the rental property, provided that when there was a reduction in that burden, the property owner must share the reduction with his tenants. The court rejected appellant rental property owners' argument that the act violated equal protection rights when reductions resulting from tax appeal judgments were excluded from the calculation for the property tax reduction, but those reductions resulting from municipal-wide revaluation of property were not. Applying the rational relation test, the court found that the act did not violate equal protection when the reason for the distinction was practicality. The court noted that the necessary information would not be available in time to calculate the tax bills. The court stated that the provision that excluded the reductions resulting from tax appeal judgments applied not only to the year for which the reduction was calculated but also to the base year.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment that upheld the constitutionality of the act. The court held that the provision that excluded property tax judgments from the calculation of the reduction, but included municipal-wide revaluation, did not violate equal protection rights. The court found that the distinction was rationally related to the proper legislative purpose of practicality.

In re Application of McCabe


A-12


Supreme Court of New Jersey


81 N.J. 462; 409 A.2d 1158; 1980 N.J. LEXIS 1305


September 24, 1979, Argued  
January 7, 1980, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] On appeal from the Appellate Division, Superior Court.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant applicants challenged the order of the Superior Court, Appellate Division (New Jersey) dismissing their application brought under N. J. Stat. Ann. § 1:7-1 et seq., seeking a declaration that supplements to the New Jersey Franchise Practices Act, N.J. Stat. Ann. 56:10-1 et seq., comprised special legislation not enacted in accordance with the notice requirements of N.J. Const., art. IV, § VII, par. 8 and N.J. Stat. Ann. 1:6-1 et seq.


OVERVIEW: Appellant applicants attempted to challenge the enactment of supplements to the New Jersey Franchise Practices Act, N.J. Stat. Ann. 56:10-1 et seq., claiming they were unconstitutional. Appellee attorney general claimed that the challenge should be dismissed because applications properly cognizable under N.J. Stat. Ann. 1:7-1 et seq. were limited to an attack only upon the machinery of enactment. The court held that N.J. Stat. Ann. 1:7-1 et seq. provided for a full hearing, witnesses, and discovery, but that it had been uniformly held that the subject matter of the inquiry involved only the mechanics of the enactment of the law and not the unconstitutional validity of the law itself. The court held that even though appellants' challenge could not be brought under N.J. Stat. Ann. § 1:7-1 et seq., N.J. Ct. R. 1:13-4(a) provided in part that if any court was without jurisdiction of the subject matter of an action or issue therein it shall, on its own initiative, order the action, with the record and all papers on file, transferred to the proper court. The court vacated the dismissal the application and transferred to the superior court, law division for further proceedings.


OUTCOME: The order of the superior court, appellate division dismissing appellant's application for a declaration that supplements to the New Jersey Franchise Practices Act were unconstitutional was vacated and the matter transferred to the superior court, law division because the application raised viable issues cognizable in that division.

State v. Lashinsky


A-171


Supreme Court of New Jersey


81 N.J. 1; 404 A.2d 1121; 1979 N.J. LEXIS 1256; 5 Media L. Rep. 1418


April 3, 1979, Argued  
July 23, 1979, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] On certification to Middlesex County Court.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant press photographer sought review of an order of the Middlesex County Court (New Jersey) that sustained his conviction for disorderly conduct, a violation of N.J. Stat. Ann. § 2A:170-29(2)(b).


OVERVIEW: Defendant press photographer observed an accident. Defendant proceeded to take pictures. A police officer requested defendant leave the area. Defendant failed to comply with the request. Defendant was arrested for disorderly conduct, a violation of N.J. Stat. Ann. § 2A:170-29(2)(b). The trial court convicted defendant. In a trial de novo, the intermediate appellate court sustained the conviction. Defendant appealed. The court found that defendant's interference did not require total physical frustration. Defendant could not have been arrested for disorderly conduct solely because the arresting officer in bad faith found defendant's behavior annoying. The court affirmed defendant's convictions because an average person would have understood the nature of the emergency and found the officer's request reasonable. Defendant's position as a member of the press did not entitle him to additional protection. While the right of the press to gather news was entitled to special protection, U.S. Const. amend. I imposed a limitation on that protection to protect important and legitimate government interests. The court affirmed because the restrictions were reasonably imposed on defendant.


OUTCOME: Judgment of the intermediate appellate court that convicted defendant press photographer of disorderly conduct was affirmed because a police officer's request for defendant to leave the scene of an accident was reasonable and an average person would have found the officer's request reasonable. Defendant, as a member of the press, was not entitled to additional First Amendment protection because the restrictions were reasonable.

Smith v. Penta


A-15


Supreme Court of New Jersey


81 N.J. 65; 405 A.2d 350; 1979 N.J. LEXIS 1262


October 16, 1978, Argued  
July 19, 1979, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Appeal dismissed by Smith v. Penta, 444 U.S. 986, 100 S. Ct. 515, 62 L. Ed. 2d 416, 1979 U.S. LEXIS 4021 (1979)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] On Certification to Superior Court, Appellate Division.
Smith v. Penta, 76 N.J. 235, 386 A.2d 860, 1978 N.J. LEXIS 776 (1978)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff, registered Republican voter, sought review of a decision of the Superior Court, Appellate Division (New Jersey) that affirmed the dismissal of plaintiff's complaint seeking declaratory and injunctive relief based on the denial of a request to register as a Democrat and to vote in the Democratic primary.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiff was a registered Republican voter. Plaintiff sought to register as a Democrat in order that she might vote in that party's primary election. Plaintiff's request was denied and she immediately filed a complaint and sought the issuance of an order to show cause why she should not be granted declaratory and injunctive relief. Plaintiff asserted that N.J. Stat. Ann. § 19:23-45, which required a 50 day party affiliation waiting period, was unconstitutional. The court affirmed holding that the state had a legitimate interest in preserving parties as viable and identifiable interest groups. The state had an interest in insuring that the results of primary elections were not undermined. If the closed primary system was to be changed, the change had to come from the legislature or the people. It could not come from the courts.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the dismissal of plaintiff registered Republican voter's complaint. The court held that if the closed primary system was to be changed, the change had to come from the legislature or from the people. The state had a legitimate interest in preserving the closed primary system.
